O, my sons, now that I have completed this book, which is the last of the sciences of medicine, by its conclusion and through its explanations and clarifications, in my view, I fulfill my duty to you in presenting this treatise, which is part of the practice known as surgery.
For surgical practice has been almost entirely abandoned in our region and time, except that the science of it still exists and its traces remain; yet we find only small descriptions of it in the books of the Ancients, which have altered much and led to errors and hesitation due to their obscure statements and weakened understanding of the art.
And it seems to me that I should revive this art through the organization of this treatise according to the way of explanation and clarification and abbreviation; and so that it comes in the forms of cautery tools and other instruments of the work as is necessary through addition of explanation, and preparation for it, which cause is essential.
But the reason why a good craftsman is not found in this field in our time is that the art of medicine is extensive, and it requires the practitioner to be well-trained in anatomy, which involves knowing the functions of the members and their forms, as well as their connections and separations; and having knowledge of the bones, nerves, and ligaments, along with their number and arrangement; and understanding the pulsating veins and the static ones, and their points of emergence.
And for this reason Hippocrates said that in name there is much talk, but in practice little, and even less in the art of the hand.
This is what we have already said at the beginning of this book; for whoever does not know what we have said about anatomy cannot be excused from falling into error through which men die, as I have seen too many of them, for some are formed in this science and get themselves out of that without knowledge and experience.
For I saw a mad physician who had incised an abscess on the neck of a woman, and he cut some arteries in the neck so that much blood flowed out, and the woman died as a result.
And I saw another physician who tried to remove a stone from a man who was in poor condition, and the stone was large. He came to him and pulled out that stone along with part of the bladder, so that on the third day, the man died.
And I had already called upon God to remove that stone, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, I knew for certain that he would die.
And I saw another physician who was paid wages by a duke of our land to perform surgery, and he attended to a black castrated man who had a fracture in his chest; and because of the doctor's ignorance, he exposed the fracture above the wound with splints and strong bandages, but did not leave room for respiration.
And he loosened it according to his desires, and then he bound it again, and after some days, he commanded us to loosen the bandage. And thus, between loosening and binding, the chest and the lung became abscessed, and he reached a state of ruin.
This is from a medieval Latin translation and it translates to:
However, once the corruption was confirmed in the limb, since it could not be restrained, and it did not cease to spread the corruption throughout the limb until the patient was in danger.
And I saw another surgeon who incised a cancerous abscess, and it became ulcerated near the armpit, until the malignancy increased greatly in the house of its lord.
The reason for this is that cancerous tumors are free of melancholic humors; therefore, it does not need to spread throughout the entire body, but rather it can be confined to a limb which can tolerate being its host.
And for this reason, my children, it is necessary for you that the operation be planned in two aspects: one is an operation associated with health, and the other is work involving significant risks.
And I have already stated this in every part of this book where there is an operation involving error and fear; therefore, it is necessary for you to heed this and avoid that, so that the pages do not find a way to speak ill and dishonor you.
And so take up your weapons with diligence and the protection of God, and approach your patients with skill and firmness.
And use the best path by God's grace to lead to health and praiseworthy success, and avoid terrible diseases that are difficult to cure, and remain armed against what causes you fear, so that hesitation in your faith and reputation does not betray you: for more remains for your glory, and even more on earth and forever for your sanctity.
Walaia (or possibly "Gualia") already says in some religious contexts: Do not treat a sick person badly, for bad doctors are not called to treat them.
And I have dedicated this book according to three chapters.
Begin the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter is ordered about the head and neck, in the forms of instruments and in the tools of cautery, and of what is necessary for the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, about drainage and puncture, and about cupping, and plasters, and extraction of arrows and similar procedures, fully chaptered and ordered in the forms of instruments.
The third chapter, about restoration and dislocation, and the treatment of fractures and similar conditions; a chapter ordered about the head and neck, and in the forms of instruments.
I will describe the quality of the actions of that cautery and its effects, and in which condition cautery should be prohibited.
Therefore, I say that the speech about the quality of the action of cautery and its effects is long and extensive, subtle by science, and hidden as a secret; and many wise people have spoken about it, and they disagree on this matter. It suffices for me to mention briefly some of their speeches, fearing lengthiness.
Therefore, I say that cauteries are applied to all complete parts universally, whether they have substance or not, except for two parts, which are tailless parts.
In the tail and dry part that has substance, it dries out; for some say that cautery is helpful for that, while others say the opposite, that cautery is not appropriate for a disease that is due to quality and dryness, since the nature of fire is quality and dryness, and it is inappropriate that a tail and dry part with something that is hot and dry be healed.
And those who say the opposite, that cautery is beneficial for every tail and dry disease that occurs in human bodies, are correct, because when you compare the body of a person and its humidity to the nature of fire, you find that the human body is fragile.
And I say according to the words of that person, that experience has already shown this to me on several occasions.
But it does not suit someone who has already been trained and exercised in the chapter of cauteries through previous practice, and who has known the diversity of human constitutions and the disposition of diseases in their seasons, as well as their causes and accidents, and the duration of them.
But in other parts, there is no need for fear on your part, especially in fragile and humid diseases, since all physicians are now in agreement about those, and they do not disagree regarding the benefit of cautery in them.
And you know, children, that the secret of healing by cautery with fire and its goodness lies in how fire is simple, its action affecting only the part being cauterized and not other parts nearby except for a slight injury.
Here is the English translation:
Keep the fire, by its height and the goodness of its substance, does nothing but superfluify [sic] and already it is known to us through experiment and through length of service in studying the art about the truth of causes; and for this I excuse myself from the length of speech And if not that it were read in my book in this pause would you secrets hidden with the fire, and the quality of its operation in bodies and of the expulsion thereof by which you expel diseases, in a philosophical demonstrative speech, which would be sharp in your ears.
And know, children, that those who neglect the time in which it is proper to perform cautery miss the best time of spring.
And I say that cautery is convenient at all times, because the harm caused by accidental circumstances of time is submerged in the benefit brought about by what is drawn from the cautery, and even more so if the cautery is for pains or necessary pains, fortifications and fistulas, which cannot tolerate delay due to fear of those where mischief may occur, as it is better to endure minor harm entering through the time.
And this does not enter your ears, or, children, what is valued only by the common people and foolish doctors is that for the illness which is cured by cautery there is not always an appropriate time, and they miss that opportunity.
And the reason is not as such because they think it is so, for cautery is merely a substitute for medicine that alters the complexion and dries up the humors which are the cause of pains.
But still, cautery is superior to medicine due to the rapidity of its effectiveness and the virtue of its operation and the greatness of its lord.
It is possible that his illness may return at some time according to the condition of the patient and the location of the illness, and its nature, and what has been prepared in his body by the accumulation of excesses in it, and what has come to him through its acquisition from food and similar causes; but if the illness for which cautery was administered is a subtle illness in a member with little excess and moisture, such as cautery of a tooth for pain and similar cases, it is possible that this pain will not return, but this happens rarely.
And the common saying is that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, and it is said by practitioners, not because those who understand it think there is no cure that benefits from medicine or any other cause before cautery, but it signifies its use as a final measure.
And the cause is contrary to that; and the understanding that cautery is the last remedy of medicine is only when we administer methods of cure for certain illnesses, and those medicines do not help, and at the end of the treatment, one administers cautery, and it benefits in such cases. And here from the perspective that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, not according to the understanding that I perceive in the common misconceptions and exaggerated practices of foolish physicians.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except for the equality of gold and its superiority in substance.
And they said that the location for cautery does not matter, which is not absolutely true, because I have experienced otherwise and found that it works differently on different bodies; in such cases, cautery with gold is good and better than with iron as they say. However, when you perform cautery using fire with gold, it is not clearly defined to you, for the hardness of gold determines its quantity, and it prepares itself to receive friction; if you apply heat over it, the gold melts due to the fire, and if you place some sheets in the fire, the craftsman can shape them according to their use.
And thus it is done that cautery is made of iron, and you should remove the pus lightly and properly according to the correctness of the procedure.
And I have already arranged this chapter on cautery after the chapters, and I have arranged those of the head among the parts, so that I alleviate the questioning of him who asks for what he wants.
The first chapter.
This cautery applies to the humidity and coldness affecting the head as an accidental condition caused by these two causes, which are the cause of stiffness and many traumatic fluxes, called herpes, from the head to the parts of the arms and ears with fluid, and much sleeping, and toothache and throat pain, and generally to all illnesses that come from coldness, such as paralysis, apoplexy, and similar conditions in those illnesses.
The form of this cautery is as follows: first, command the patient to evacuate with a laxative medicine that cleanses the head, for three or four nights, depending on what the strength of the patient requires and their age and usual habits.
And then command him to shave his head with a razor, and then have him sit between the sheets on a padded bench and place his head on his hands.
And then place the palm of your hand near the spot above the shaved nose, between the eyebrows. But when the heat of your hand has reached that spot, mark that place with ink, and then apply the olive wood cautery, which is made as follows:
And then pause there upon the marked spot with the probe, in such a way that it descends, by which the wall of the place may be gradually pressed back, and you turn it.
And then lift your hand gently and look at the place, and if you see any amount of the head bone uncovered, as much as the amount of a grain of wheat, now raise your hand, and if not return your hand to that tool, or another like it, if it is cooled, until you can feel the bone which I have told you about.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it on the spot and let it be for three days.
And then pause on the inner edge and let it be inside until the fire scar is destroyed. And then treat the place with a quadrifarmaceutical ointment until it is healed.
And it is said that when the wound has become clean and uncovered, the sooner the arm is removed, the better and more beneficial it is.
And some of them say that the wound should be cauterized down to the bone, and the cautery should be applied until it adheres to some part of the bone.
And then remove what is adhered to the bone, and then it should be treated.
And others say that with the cautery it should reach the deepest part, until it presses strongly against the bone until the bone melts, and this is the method of alpechini (cauterization in small increments): And they believe that vapor from the head is expelled from that place.
And let the wound be treated for a long time until it is healed and has consolidated.
I have not seen these two types of cautery used generally on everyone out of fear; and leave that, I think, it is better for the health to avoid them, when possible; for the brain is easily injured by breaking the continuity of the skull just as we have testified in other parts of the body, and especially when the constitution of the patient is weak.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better, in my opinion; and with that one can act more safely for health.
Chapter Second.
On Cautery of the Head.
When chronic and prolonged pain occurs throughout the head, and this condition persists despite using herbs, cloths, caps, plasters, poultices, cats, hens, and similar treatments, especially if cautery has already been applied but without success for any of these causes, then consider whether the patient's constitution is strong and not weak or delicate. If it is robust and there is no great risk, proceed with another form of cautery below that one slightly. And then cauterize that area over the crown of their head with cautery until the pain is relieved. Cut open the pain and expose the bone in the amount we have described, and cauterize that area with a cautery at the back of the head in the place called "vas," which is where the vein is located. And be cautious in this, and do not expose the bone, for the patient will experience great pain from this, different from the pain of other head cauteries.
Here is the cautery in its proper place, for the cautery with which you cauterize the horns of the head and the occiput is finer than that with which you cauterize the middle part of the head; and this is the form of it.
Chapter three.
On the cautery for non-ancient inflammation.
When severe pain occurs in the part of the head or persistent pain within the eye, it should be treated with medicines that cleanse the head, and the other cure which I have mentioned for diseases should be administered; and if this does not benefit, then cauterization is done in two ways: either with a sharp burning medicine or with iron.
But the cautery with a burning medicine is that you take a piece of cloth, soak it in oil, and then hold both ends of it. And then you should cut the area of the pain on the temple with a lancet until it is done properly below the hairline, so that it is hidden.
And then press on it with a cloth for about three hours; and afterwards untie it and remove the oil, and leave the wound open for two or three days; and then apply pressure to it submerged in butter until the area becomes red.
And then cure it with an ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines which I have written about in the second treatise on burning medicines.
Here is the cautery according to this method: fire for the cautery of which this is the form:
and it is called a key cautery, because the head of it is in the shape of a clove, in which there is some curvature, and at its middle part there is a small prominence.
And then place it on the site of the pain, and hold your hand — so that the artery which is below is not adhered to — and turn the iron slightly by slight degrees with speed.
And if the amount that adheres due to the nature of the tissue is as much as half of it, raise your hand so that the artery below is not adhered to, so that there is no flow of blood.
And then moisten a piece of cloth in salt water and place it on the site, and leave it for three days; and then place a piece of cloth with borther (possibly referring to a type of poultice or bandage), and then care for it with an ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize at the end of the ulna according to the cautery method, and guard against cutting the artery in this proper but non-traditional procedure.
Chapter four.
On the cautery of an ancient ulcer.
When you treat an ulcer as we have said in the previous treatment and as we have mentioned in the divisions of diseases, and if it does not improve with that treatment, and at times due to the disease what we have said about the first cautery with medicine or the cautery with fire is insufficient, then you should ignite the ulnar cautery until it becomes white-hot. Place it on the site of the pain along a line the length of which is half the width of your finger, or slightly more; and then move your hand once and forcefully so that it cuts the artery and reaches into the bone.
However, you must guard against the continuation of the jawbone, which is damaged due to chewing, and that the muscle attached to it and the nerve which moves it are not affected, so that spasm does not occur.
And if it is about the care and caution of the flow of blood from the artery that is severed, because there is fear in its severing, especially for one who does not know what they are doing and is not diligent and experienced; and leave the operation to someone at the beginning.
And here I will come to the reminder of the management of blood flow from an artery according to the usual method, in its place in this book.
And if you see that this disease does not respond to these cauteries and the patient's condition is tolerable, cauterize at the appropriate place on the head as we have recounted, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you do so, administer the cautery that we have mentioned in the chapter on the rupture of an artery using a cautery with two edges, and this is a better cautery and more effective.
Chapter 5.
On the cautery for ear pain.
When pain in the ear occurs due to irritation and is treated with laxative causes and other treatments as I have mentioned in the division, and the pain does not subside, now apply the cautery, and this one has the name puncta, and here is its form:
And bring the puncta close to the fire, and move it around the entire ear as a wheel turns, or in the vicinity of each affected area if the pain is localized there. Lengthen the cautery of the earlobe bit by bit until you have marked the place with it.
And if you cauterize those points throughout the entire earlobe, and wherever it is necessary, and then treat the places until they are healed.
Chapter VI.
On the Cauterization of the Turtle.
The tumor which is cured without cautery is nothing but of the kinds which come from phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of diseases.
Avoid cautery for the kind that arises from dryness or spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that kind of tumor with scarifications and capillary purgatives and gargles, and your treatment does not succeed, then it is necessary to cauterize the disease with three cauteries: one at the root of the ear, the second a little distance from there in the temple area, and the third at the junction of two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side of the affected part, because softening only occurs in the part that appears healthy.
The method of cautery is that you cauterize there with the cautery on the right side of the upper part of the ear, then a little above the horn of the head, and in the temple area another, whose length should be according to the length of your thumb, and then place your hand with the cautery until half the thickness of the tumor is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is specific for the cutler's tool, the form of which is set forth below. However, it is even more subtle that you should see it slightly like this; and it is necessary to use a knife instead of excessive coarseness, just a little.
And then treat the area with what was previously described until it is healed.
Chapter VII.
On cauterization for ancient apoplexy.
When apoplexy is ancient and you treat it as we have said and do not cause fever in the patient, then cauterize it with four cauteries: on top of all the temples of their head; one cauterization at the middle part of the head, as we have said; and one cauterization in the nape. And the method of the cauterizations is done as mentioned above.
And it has already been cauterized over the mouth of the stomach; and this is better; and soon it will be cured as you proceed.
Chapter VIII.
On cauterization for oblivion done with flame.
Give the patient large doses at the beginning and pills to purify the mind.
And then shave the patient's head completely and place on their nape the plaster described in the chapter on plasters.
And place it several times, for this is the method of cauterization. And do this according to the order I have already mentioned.
And if this treatment is effective; otherwise, then perform three cauteries on the rear part of their head, which should be positioned from the most upper part of the head down to the part closest to the neck. And place about the thickness of a finger between each cauterization, and then treat the cauterizations as described.
If you want to apply it for the lesser harm, then now make the cautery in the middle location; and if you wish to apply more intense treatment, now cauterize that over both horns.
Apply the treatment inside where it will heal; and let the cautery be olive-shaped according to the form given before.
Chapter IX.
On the cautery for parasites and the softening of the entire body.
Proceed as you would in the cleansing of the head with scissors and according to what has been said; then shave the head of the patient, and subsequently cauterize that in the middle part of the head, and with a cautery over all parts of the head, and with a cautery on the back part of it, and with three cauteries along the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary for the illness of softening the body to apply that and the patient can tolerate it when the illness is severe, then cauterize that with four cauteries along the vertebrae of the back, and finally with a cautery inside where it will be covered by the skin, and increase the heat gradually. Then apply the treatment inside where it will heal, and let this be an olive-shaped cautery.
Chapter X.
On the cautery for epilepsy.
An epileptic is not cauterized except when the epilepsy is due to phlegm.
And therefore, you should cleanse the brain of the patient at the beginning with scissors and according to the other treatment that has been mentioned in the division when the illness is severe and the patient can tolerate the exception of medicines.
And if it is a child who cannot endure the medicines, at that time administer guar gum and masticatory substances to cleanse the mouth, for several days, with purification of the foods.
And then shave the head of the patient, and then cauterize one with a cautery in the middle part of the head according to the previous method, and another cauterization at the back of the head, and on each side of the head it is cauterized. And if the patient is strong and can endure it, then cauterize with the cauteries that I have mentioned, having paralysis and relaxation of the body over the cervical vertebrae and the thoracic vertebrae.
And let it be an olive cauterization as was described previously.
But if the patient is a child, then use a gentle cauterization according to this method.
Chapter XI.
On Cauterization for Melancholia.
When the causes of melancholia are due to corrupted and flaccid humidity, then cauterize with the cauteries that we have mentioned while inducing paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholia is an excess inclining towards melancholy, and the body of the patient is humid, then give him to drink what purifies his brain according to what was mentioned earlier in the division.
And then shave the head of the patient, and then apply a well-made poultice over the circle, and subsequently place it in the middle part of the head.
Let the wound be sixty days old, and let it be treated from all sides.
And then apply a liniment; and it is more suitable to use the grease of sheep. And warm that over the fire with moderate heat prepared according to measure, which the patient can endure when it is applied. And then place it in the proper location on the head in a circle, and let it be rubbed in.
And do this once a week for the wound, following another regimen until it is healed; or if you prefer, cauterize that with small points as needed, but have your hand with a cautery so arranged: this is a special kind of cautery that moistens the brain with equilibrium.
And then apply that powder soaked in grease or in chicken fat.
Chapter XII.
On the cautery of water descending in the stomach.
When it has been determined for you the beginning of water in the stomach by the signs which I have mentioned in the division, then begin and give the patient something to drink that purifies the head, and protect him from all humidity.
And have him sweat in a warm bath for several days.
And then command him to shave his head, and cauterize that with a cautery in the proper location on the head; and then cauterize that with two cauteries over two temples if it is the beginning of water rising in both sides of the stomach, or on one side if it is the beginning of water in one stomach.
Cut away all the veins and arteries that you leave for the ligature; and place the cautery at a distance in the region of the temples.
And guard against bleeding when it is cauterized; and if you see any bleeding, remove the cautery and use some medicine that is available to you.
And I will come to the knowledge which is in the extraction of arteries and their ligation and caution against bleeding.
And sometimes it is cauterized on the neck below two bones with two cauteries placed rearward to produce the effect.
Chapter XIII.
On the cauterization of old wounds.
When there are tears from the eyes that are old and constantly flow through veins and arteries which are in obvious places on the head, and it is verified that this is a superfluity of cold phlegm, then one cauterizes that place with the cautery which we have recounted in the chapter on how water collects in the eyes, and one cautery in the middle part of the head and two cauteries at the base of the skull below the temples, and two cauteries at the base of the skull below the bones. And if you need an addition [correction: addition likely refers to a medicinal or surgical adjunct], then cauterize that area along the entire side of the eye above the extremity of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter XIV.
On Cauterization for Nasal Odor.
When you treat that which we have mentioned in the division and it does not benefit from your treatment, then prepare and give to the sick person cock's blood to drink for three nights; and afterwards shave his head, and cauterize that place with a moderate cautery using an olive cautery. And afterwards cauterize that place with a clove cautery, and use two cauteries above the two eyebrows behind the hair a little bit.
And be careful that you do not burn that area with the cautery.
And this is the form of the clove cautery.
Chapter XV.
On Cauterization for Softening the Eyelids.
When the eyelid of the eye is softened by disease or moisture, then cauterize that eyelid with a cautery like this lunar one; and this is its form:
And if you do, cauterize that area above both eyebrows a little with two cauteries throughout, and let them extend to the temples. And let the length of each cautery be according to the quantity of the eyebrow.
And do not press too hard with your hand on the cauteries, but rather so that one-third of the eyelid is adhered.
Chapter XVI.
On the cautery of the eyelid when the hairs turn inward and pinch the eye.
When the hairs of the eye turn inward such that they pinch the eye, the cautery for them is according to two methods: either it is done with a cautery or with combustible medicine.
If you use a cautery with fire, command the patient before that to shave off their hairs as those of the world are shaved, such that they are extended and equalized; and if they pinch near their origin, then you should bandage the eyes tightly so that nothing enters until the hair has grown back.
When they have grown back and are equalized, then place the head of the patient in your lap, and gently apply a tent over the eyelids using a sign similar to the shape of a maple leaf, with the beginning of the sign near the hairs.
And after this pause, cut it open and insert into an eggshell or into a poultice of bran on the wound.
And then apply fire to the cautery, which has this shape.
And then cauterize over the figure that is marked, little by little, in small strokes, until the surface of the skin, which resembles a myrtle leaf, is completely burned through properly.
And the sign of health in this procedure is that you see the eyelid of the wound already contracted and the hairs raised above the puncture of the wound.
And then raise your hand and lay it still for three days, and after that pause over the cut until the scar is reddened. And then care for it with dressing until it is healed.
But if the hairs return due to some cause after their removal and the eyelid is softened, then perform the cautery on that area again as you did initially.
But if the hairs grow back on the eyelid, then cauterize that area until it is raised to its natural position and dressed so that the hairs do not irritate the wound.
But the cautery with a burning medicine should be such that you instruct the patient to pull out the hairs until they are elongated and then dressed, and afterwards make the shape of a myrtle leaf from paper or cloth.
And then take known soap and one ounce of live herbs, or something similar, and mix these ingredients well, applying it quickly so that it is not left on too long; then spread this mixture over the paper made in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and place it on both eyes; and put an eggshell under the wound cut open, and have the patient’s head covered with your hand.

And if not, return to the medicine in the place where you did not apply the medicine before until your operation is evened out and the swelling subsides.

Chapter XVII.
On the cautery treatment of a fistula that is in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat fistula with the water we have mentioned in the divisions of diseases and do not confine your treatment, then it is necessary that you cauterize it according to this method: command the patient to place his head or thigh in your hand, and hold the head of a cautery between your hands in such a way that it is neither too much nor agitated with respect to the head.
And place cotton in the wound or on the skin above it.
And then light the cautery, which has this form:
which is hollow, just as it has the shape of a reed or the quill of a bird, with one end through which the cautery is made.
And if you wish, it can penetrate into the other end. And if you want it to be as thin as a thread.
But the one that is concave is better for your work.
And then press the fistula if it is open, and draw out the pus and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the putrid matter from it.
And then place the cautery, which should be well heated, over it. Hold it at a distance from the patient's face so that your hand or the patient is not disturbed, and then place the cautery on the skin around the wound and cauterize it.
And if at the beginning of your cauterization it penetrates to the bone, that is good; and if not, then repeat the cauterization once or twice if necessary. Then leave it like that for three days, and afterwards place cotton around it and apply a drying ointment until it heals.
If after eleven days it has not healed, then apply acute corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed according to what is convenient in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula which the ancients first described: enter into the location of the fistula and make it open; then apply in that fissure finely powdered material, whose form matches its shape. And put into it about one ounce of molten lead; and hold with your hand gently but firmly so that not much pus is lost because otherwise the molten lead will reach the flesh of that area; and you should place over the surface of the fistula cloth infused in egg white or water, for lead adheres to the location of the fistula and heals it miraculously.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said about cauterization and treatment, it is good; but if not, then it is necessary to administer traction through the nose and reduce the fistula to the course of the nose as it will come into its proper place.

This is about the treatment of ruptures in other parts of the abdomen, and at the beginning, you want them not to spread, so cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size, and it should be as thick as the third part of it; then treat that area as we have said, and it is sufficient.
And some earlier ones said that the rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery in this form, as it is marked with a tenta:
and draw a line that you follow along the latitude in the flanks above the rupture, and another line above it; then place your hand with a nail-shaped cautery, but the first cautery is quicker and better.
Chapter XLVI.
On the Cauterization of Twists.
When pain occurs in some members due to falling or impact and persists for a long time without being relieved by medicine, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And the size of the cautery for each member should be according to its size and smallness, its strength and weakness, and the location of the twist and pain.
If it heals with the first cautery, that is good; but if not, then repeat the cautery. For it is common for such pains to be punctured in a member next to the one affected, so you must achieve this with the cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter XLVII.
On cauterization for leprosy.
Some lepers benefit greatly from cauterization, but it is not much help for the type of leprosy that results from the putrefaction of flesh and melancholia.
And therefore, when you wish to cauterize that person, consider whether the leprosy is commissary and can be cured with what we have said in the division, and do not proceed if it is not so, and fear that the malady will cause corruption to encompass the whole body. Then cauterize that person on the head with a hot iron: place one at the middle of the head, and another further back; then over the forehead at the end of the hair; two on both temples; and one at the back, near the nape or neck.
Lastly, your cauterization should go a little into the skin to create a small indentation, so that the scales are displaced and the expulsion of thick vapors is eased; cauterize over the ulcer as required.
And if the leprosy has already spread over the body and is clearly manifest, then you should cauterize that person with those irons which we mentioned for the head; and use an iron on the tip of the nose, two on each cheekbone, two on both sides of the neck vertebrae, ten on the lower back vertebrae, a large one near the hip bones, one on each hip bone, two on each thigh, one on each side of the thighs, two on each elbow and one on each side of the elbows, two on each wrist and two on each muscle, and two on each tendon of their feet; cauterize that person over all the joints of the fingers and toes, and on each heel of their feet, and on each thigh bone.
And guard against the nerves, especially those on the heel, that you do not burn them.
And in some cases, it is cauterized with one cautery on the bone of the pubis and another on the mouth of the stomach, and another on the spleen.
And know that the more you adjust the cauteries, the more beneficial and effective they are; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery as much as the observers do, because the body of the patient is already numbed.
And it is appropriate that the cauteries be large or few according to the members and joints, depending on the nature of the tools used.
And properly care for the cautery with flour mixed with honey, and continue the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter XLVIII.
On Cauterization for Inducing Stupor.
When a limb is numb or asleep and is treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, but does not heal, then you should cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the size of the limb.
And let the heat of the cautery enter the depth of the skin a little. And properly care for it with ointment until it is healed.
And in some cases, numbness is cauterized on parts that affect the muscles and joints, and on the nerves that control that limb, to relieve the numbness.
Let no one undertake this except one who is skilled in the anatomy of the members and of the emergence of the nerves throughout the moving body.
Chapter XLIX.
On cauterization of ulcers that become inflamed.
When ulcers are old and do not respond to any of the ointments of medicine, then you should cauterize over them with something that will change their appearance slightly so that they become redder and their color is altered.
And for that, apply a poultice made of flour of oats and lentils with rose oil and leaves of arnica, and the blood of dove or the blood of robin, a part of each, and all mixed together. Then place a cloth over it until it is healed.
Chapter L.
On cauterization of cancer.
When cancer begins and you wish to remove it, then cauterize around it with a circular instrument as it is surrounded.
And the wise say that it should be cauterized with the deepest cauterization in the middle area; but I have not seen this done, for I fear that it will become ulcerated, and I have observed this happening several times.
And it is safer to cauterize around it in a circular manner as we have said, or with multiple cauterizations.
Chapter LI.
On cautery for hemorrhoids.
When hemorrhoids are delayed in maturation, and this is due to the excess moisture in that area, or because of the patient's condition, being old and weak with little blood, or due to the time; if you wish to hasten their maturation, then cauterize around them with small punctures; and afterwards let it rest so that it may mature quickly.
But if you want to perforate with the cautery, then prepare the cautery which has this shape; and place it in the middle of the hemorrhoid until it penetrates the tissue.
And if the cautery is applied to a place further away so that the arm is more comfortable; and then take care of it until it heals.

On cautery for corrosive treatment.
Corrodent is a corrosive condition in the limb, which corrodes as if fire were consuming dry wood.
Therefore, if you find the corrosive condition in a place where it can tolerate cautery with fire, then prepare pointed cauteries, small and large as is appropriate for the location of the corrosive condition.
And then cauterize it from all sides until you eradicate all the corrosion and leave no cause for it to remain.
Leave that for three days, and then pause on the places cauterized thrice with oil mixed in until all the scars are completely eradicated and all corruption is removed.
And then treat that with something that promotes flesh growth.
If after three days the flesh grows healthy with no corruption, that is good; but if not, then at this point repeat the cautery on what remains in the corrupted places.
And for corroding, some cases are treated with an acute medicine, because that is the method instead of cautery. However, cautery with fire acts quickly and effectively.
And I have already mentioned the treatment with acute medicine in the division, and so use that when you see improvement.
Chapter LIII.
On Cauterization of Joints and Other Similar Areas.
Often in the lower parts of the foot there is a sickness called a joint, which is caused by swelling and inflammation that affects the foot; and cauterization in such cases follows two methods, namely cautery with fire and cautery with hot water.
However, cautery with fire is done by heating the concave cauterizing instrument, similar to a small wheel, which is made of iron according to the size of the joint in all parts, and it should be finely polished.
And then place the heated instrument on the joint, and then move your hand with the cautery around the circumference of the joint until the cautery reaches the depth of the joint. Let him be kept for three days and then let bleeding begin. And apply a poultice of wild mallows and salt to it, and let the poultice remain on for one night, for thereby it will be eradicated from its roots.
And then treat the area with a healing ointment until it is healed.
And if you find non-conversion occurring, and spots frequently appearing on the surface of the body, especially in the masses and the glands, then you should administer cautery with water. Take a small tube made of iron or copper or tin and place it over the affected area; then pour a little hot water into the tube, hold it tightly, and rotate it under pressure from your hand, so that the edge of the tube touches the root of the affected part. Wait for about an hour as the hot water draws the moisture out through the root of the affected area; and after a short time, remove it, because then the spot with its roots will be eradicated.
And do this with each of them in turn until you have addressed all such spots on the body.
And then treat the pus where the roots of the spots have been eradicated with the water from ointments that promote flesh growth.
Chapter LIII.
On Cauterization of Tremors.
When you find a tremor due to irritation of the nerves or from fever caused by friction, or from some similar cause, then it is necessary that you cauterize it with four cauteries or five on the vertebrae of the back, between each vertebra with one cauterization; and at the feet, and above the stomach, use an olive-shaped cauterization.
And when the shaking subsides and hastens the health of the patient, it rubs.

On the cautery of white spots on the body.
When on the body there appear white spots and pus-filled blisters which are made of thick, corrupted matter; therefore, at the beginning when they appear, it should be cauterized over the entire head of the fistula with a fine cautery made from myrtle wood, the end of which is extended over fire; or with long rosemary twigs; or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes the cautery is applied immediately at the beginning of its expulsion according to this method; and it is not feared, and the excess is removed by doing so, and they heal from them.

Chapter LVI.
On the cautery of a bleeding caused by an accidental cut to an artery.
Often when a bleeding from an artery that has been cut occurs due to the disease that lies within, or due to the beginning of abscesses or cauterization of the limb, and similar situations, it is difficult to stop that.
Therefore, when such a situation occurs, immediately apply your hand to the opening of the artery, and pause over it with your thumb pressed firmly to stop the bleeding until the flow of blood ceases.
And place in the fire the cauteries of olive wood, both small and large, in great number, and blow upon them until they are well alight.
And immediately take one of them, either small or large, according to the location and condition where the artery is ruptured, and place it quickly upon the vein before your hand trembles, so that the cautery becomes heated and constricts the blood.
And if the blood is expelled from the opening of the artery when you move your hand and take out the cautery, then immediately take another one quickly from those cauteries which are prepared in the fire, and thus do not cease to do this one after another until the blood is constricted.
And be careful that you do not burn the nerve that is here, and that it does not cause other harm.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, it is then impossible to constrict the blood in those cases, especially when the artery is large, unless you use one of four methods: either by using cautery as we have mentioned, or by extraction when it cannot be extracted, for when it is extracted the extremities are burned and the blood is constricted, or let it be treated with honey or a bandage with strong pressure, or let them be placed upon medicines whose virtue is to restrict the blood; and they should be strengthened with a pad and appropriate compression.
But he who administers proper restraint with bandages and pressure using cloths or by positioning burning substances and similar methods should do so slowly.
But if this situation befalls someone and there is no physician present or medicine available, then gently press the index finger on the opening of that vein as we have said, and apply strong pressure until the blood stops from the main part of the wound, and do not move your finger over the artery; and sprinkle cold water over it, which has great chilliness, to congeal and thicken the blood; and then determine whether cautery or medicine is necessary.

Chapter VII.
On the treatment of blocked ears.
Sometimes infants are born from their mothers' wombs with unopened ears, and this also occurs in some adults, where the ear canal is blocked by infection or flesh growing there; and this blockage sometimes affects the narrow part of the ear canal which cannot be seen clearly, and sometimes it is in the outer parts.
And when it occurs in the narrow part of the ear canal, which cannot be seen clearly, it is in the most difficult positions for healing.

And if the blockage is in the narrow part of the ear canal, then take a thin needle and slightly heat it over fire, and then insert it into the ear canal.
And thus if you have seen that the swelling occurs where the patient finds ease by lying on it, because of the gravity of the ear, it forces the oppression of what is below; and guard yourself so as not to plague the nerves of the ear.
And then pause in the ear a plug of what you made according to the quantity of the cloth which you wrapped in Egyptian cloth; and do this for several days until you are sure of the consolidation of the injury; or take something and insert it into water and powder it over it three times and use it. And if you see the ear already inflamed, then you must remove that plug and replace it with another plug which you wrap in wax mixed with rose oil until the inflammation subsides; and afterward care for it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then you submerge a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use another treatment that restricts the flow of blood, which is described in the chapter on distillation.
Chapter VIII.
On the treatment of boils that occur on the eyelids.
Boils that occur on the eyelids sometimes become moist, and at other times they are dry.
Therefore, you should have these available either in tin containers or in ounce quantities, and then scrape them with the edge of their rasps, and afterward place them on the affected areas three times.
But if pain with bleeding occurs, then you should cauterize those areas with a fine ancient cautery.
The best cautery is applied after incisions and wounds have been examined to see if there is any cause for inflammation remaining; and when it has been applied with heat, those inflammations are consumed and do not return.
Chapter IX.
Treatment for ailments that affect the eyelids.
Inflamed and hard swellings that occur in the eyelids due to some cause are compared in their strength and hardness to serious ailments and are therefore called serious.
And it is an accumulation of thick moisture in the eyelid, both above and below.
Therefore, the treatment for this is to observe whether the swelling has appeared on the surface of the eyelid and spread throughout, and if so, the cause is superficial.
Then make an incision along the length of it, and subsequently drain it thoroughly until all of it is evacuated, and then dress it with some ointment and bandage it.
And if the condition is severe, then you adjust it with stitches; and take care of it until it is healed.
And if it is minor, you will not have to do much for it because the warmth of the eye restores and heals it.
And if the swelling is spreading to the inner parts of the eyelid, you should examine its surface internally. Then dress the eyelid and apply ointment to the swelling, without making an incision if it is not necessary, and press it thoroughly from all sides. If the eyelid needs to be incised to lengthen it, note that there is no need for such action unless absolutely necessary.
At the beginning, wash the wound with salt water; and treat the area with liquid that will cause it to heal until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
About the treatment of a tumor above the eye on the eyelid.
A tumor is grayish and is generated in the layers of the upper eyelid, and it occurs more often in children, aggravating their eyes and causing them pain; they cannot look at the brightness of the sun because tears accumulate there.
And because of this, you often see them lying on their faces all the time and sleeping with their faces down; and thus, their lower eyelids are covered by moist eyebrows, which have become inflamed, and there is an apparent swelling near the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel a tumor within it.
And one method of treatment is that the patient places their head in your lap, and you place a cloth over them and make a circle according to the size it contains arthernach from all sides, and then place that on top and press with your fingers all around so that a tumor aggregates at the center of the circle, and then apply moisture with spatum incident on the fissure along the latitude, and the fissure should not be larger than the one made in flavotomy.
But in the incision you must cut through the skin entirely until you reach the tumor, for in severe cases, you proceed to remove the tumor through a fissure according to the shape of white gristle.
Then extract it from the cloths in which you have wrapped them, and roll it between your right and left hands until it is dispersed.
And be careful not to insert it into the fissure so that the pus does not reach the wound and damage it.
But if it does not appear to you appropriate at the beginning of the fissure, then insert into the fissure a small piece with ease so that it can be easily processed; and subsequently extract it as we have said; and then immerse the cloths in warm water and vinegar and place them over the location and sprinkle them with powder.
And some people use salt and apply it in the deepest part of the fissure so that what remains is free from moisture.
And then take care of it until it is healed.
But if an abscess occurs in the area, now treat it with soft plasters until it is healed.

Chapter XII.
On the elevation of stinging hairs in the eye with an awl when the hair is hard.
When there are unnatural hairs, hard and rough, that sting the eyes, then the operation is that you take a fine awl and place it at the base of each hair in a delicate manner, and then adjust the ends of them carefully. And let the length of the strips be equal to the quantity of one palm.
And then prepare another fine strip below it briefly, and use the ends of that one if you wish.
And then raise the patient's head with a pillow, and place it near a light source so that your work is clear, for this is a delicate and precise operation.
And then insert the needle into the root of the hair that needs to be extracted, and let it penetrate the eyelid quickly before you remove it from above the natural hair.
And then pull the hair out from above and insert it into an instrument, and let there be one or two or three hairs and no more.
And then pull your hand away so that the hair is removed along with the instrument from the eyelid, leaving the natural hair.
But if the instrument is pulled away and the hair is not removed, pull the instrument away from the surrounding area toward the strip that you have prepared for it, so that the instrument is cleanly withdrawn without the hair.
And look at that with the eye's vision, for now you will withdraw the instrument and the strip, and wash the eye with water, and leave it covered for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and grows back on healthy flesh.
And if the hair is shorter than desired, now attach a long hair from among the natural hairs to it, and raise it up with that one, or leave it for some time so that it grows longer in place, and then raise it.
Chapter XIII.
On the treatment of a wound that occurs above the eyelid.
When there is a wound above the eyelid, and it contracts when the eyelid is closed, either to the right or to the left side, due to poor healing of a wound or incision or cauterization, it is called leporine. This wound can be either natural or accidental, caused by improper healing of wounds, incisions, or cauterizations, or similar malpractice.
The method for the procedure is that you should divide that contraction and separate its edges, then place between them a piece of linen cloth, and bind it there until it heals.
And it is not appropriate to use treatments for it that dry or constrict, because if you do this, the wound will return even worse and never heal.
Use treatments that soften, such as fenugreek; boil the seeds and leaves in water, or dissolve some myrobalan in it, which should be liquefied with some oil, then soak a piece of cloth in this mixture and apply it.
The essence of your treatment is that you should ensure nothing remains unhealed according to its original form as it was at the beginning.
And this operation we have described is merely a correction of a wound that does not return to its original form as it was before.
Chapter XIV.

This hematoma which occurs below the eyelid, which is properly called a hematoma; and it can be either natural or accidental.

Chapter XV.
On the adherence of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this consolidation occurs in some individuals such that the eyelid is adhered above to the sclera, and its movement and natural functions are restricted, then you must introduce a blunt instrument below the eyelid, elevate it with it, or extend it to the root.

Chapter XVII.
On the incision of styes and of what is born from flesh tightly attached to the eye.
