Chapter XXVI.
On sewing up the nose and lips and ear when continuity is lost due to ulcer or similar condition.
Know that when there is a rupture of continuity in one of these cartilages, few will undertake such an operation except in some men.
Therefore, when there is any reason for one of these conditions, consider whether it is a fresh ulcer with its own blood, and then you should approximate the two edges of the wound with stitches, and subsequently treat it until it heals.
And if the edges of the rupture in continuity are already separated, and each edge is healthy, then you should scrape away each part of its apparent edge until blood comes out. And then approximate the two edges with stitches and strengthen them, and over these sprinkle dragon's blood powder and thrisse (a type of grass), and place on top a piece of mastic plaster or another consolidating plaster, and leave it for two days or three, and then remove it and change the plaster, and continue treating with an ointment until it is healed.
The method of suturing is that you add the solution of continuity or with needles as we have recounted in the suturing of the abdomen, or add the solution of continuity with thread as you do and make known.
Chapter XXVII.
On the extraction of those causes that occur in the two lips in the privates.
Many times in some men in the private parts of their lips appear hard abscesses resembling goose fat, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Then it is necessary that you turn the lips and incise each of those hard areas from all sides, and immediately after the incision fill the place with finely chopped wool until the blood has clotted, and then wash it with warm water and treat the area with something astringent until it is healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
On the incision of flesh that is adhered to the gums.
Often there appears on the gum flesh that is adhered to it, which the ancients called albulus. Then it is necessary that you suspend that with a thread or needle and incise it along its root, and allow the pus to run out or the blood.
And then apply to the area finely chopped wool or other astringent powders or exiccative substances.
And if that flesh returns, then incise it again, and cauterize it, for after the cautery it will not return.
Chapter XXIX.
On the Reason for Pulling Teeth with Iron.
When on the surface of the teeth in both the inner and outer parts and between the gums are sharp, rough patches or stains, some of which are black and others are yellowish or green, causing corruption to occur at the gum, and thus the teeth become stained.
And therefore it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your thighs and place his head on your thigh, and rasp the teeth in which those rough patches appear until no such cause remains, and this will remove the blackness and yellowness and other stains so they may be cleansed.
If this is done as described at the beginning of the rationale, it is good; if not, repeat the procedure on those teeth on another day and on the second and third days until you reach the final stage as you intend.
And know that the teeth have the need for various tools and figures of different shapes according to how they are prepared for your operation. And thus the razor used to rasp the inner part of the tooth is different from the razor used to rasp between the teeth according to other forms, and these forms of razors you should prepare as you will see shortly:
Chapter XXX.
On the Extraction of Teeth.
When you treat the tooth of its pain with all diligence and delay in total extraction, for there is no cause that can replace the place it occupies once it is extracted, as it is a substance of high quality that cannot be compensated for by its removal.
When the patient complains that a tooth must be extracted, you should firmly be certain which is the painful tooth, for often the pain deceives the patient and he says it is in a healthy tooth, and if you extract that one, the pain will not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is removed, as we have already seen by the experience of barbers.
Therefore, you should be certain which is the painful tooth, and once you know that, it is necessary to scarify around the tooth with something spatula-like in which there is some virtue until it dissolves from the tooth and gum completely, and then remove it with your said tools or with fine forceps little by little until it comes out.
And then grasp it with strong forceps for good leverage or firm hold, and place the head of the patient between your knees so that you have a secure position without hurting him, and then pull the tooth straight in such a way that you do not break it, nor cause more pain to the patient.
And if it is not dislodged, then take one of those instruments and place it beneath it on all sides and exert the removal as was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is fractured or broken, then you should cover that fracture with a cloth and secure it with a thin thread so that it does not break further during your constriction on it with forceps.
And you should work carefully around the scarification on all sides of the gum and guard against your strength so that you do not break it or damage any part of it, and return to the patient with caution for the sake of ensuring that what follows is better than the initial pain.
And be careful that you do not perform what ignorant barbers do in their rashness and audacity regarding the removal of [tumors], since they do not administer what we have recommended; for often they cause serious illnesses to come upon people, which are harder to cure, and the roots of them remain uncured completely, or partially, or that you extract it along with some bones from the jaw as we have often testified.
And after the removal of [the tumor], the mouth should be washed with warm water or salt water.
And if there is bleeding due to this, as often happens, then apply some quantity of sugar and sprinkle it over the wound, and if that does not help, cauterize.
The shape of the forceps and holding them skillfully with which the tooth is pulled at the beginning should be long, short-handed, and thick, so that they do not bend when you need to work with them.
And they should be like thick handles inside so that when you grasp them, they do not bend, and their extremities should be short.
And they should be made of Indian iron in the form of straight extremities, and at the ends of those there should be teeth of which some fit into others and grasp by strong constriction, and sometimes the extremities of those are shaped like the beak of a bird called a stork, which is strong and has a firm grip.
Chapter XXXI.
On the removal of teeth and the extraction of the bones of the locked jaw.
When you must perform the extraction of a tooth that is rotten for some reason, then wait on the place cut around it for one day or two until the area becomes softened; and afterwards insert these or use tools whose ends are shaped like the beak of a bird called ciconia or guanta, which have the shape of forceps:
The ends of them should be made in such a way as with a file or a rasp.
And if it does not obey to you when using these forceps or tools, then wait on the root and cut away all the flesh and insert the instrument called atali, which is a small lever whose shape is as follows:
And it should be of short thick length, and not enclosed such that it is rotten.
Then if by this the root is exposed, it is good; and if not, help yourself with these other instruments of which these are the forms: the form of the first instrument is a triangular end in which there is some thickness:
The form of the second instrument is that it be triangular and of even finer end.
And with this instrument, which has two prongs of the following form, you will assist: and with other instruments which will be mentioned for scraping teeth; because it is useful to use this instrument that resembles a small spoon, and this is its form:
with a triangular end that is bent or short, which has some thickness, and is not enclosed such that it is rotten.
And know that the instruments for the teeth are made in a similar manner to the other instruments.
And the skilled and subtle artisan finds for himself according to what he understands from the operation and the various diseases; because there are diseases about which the ancients did not say or leave a proper instrument, due to the forms of diverse types.
But if the bone of the jaw or other bones in the mouth is fractured, or if you need to apply pressure, then proceed carefully using appropriate instruments and forceps, as we have described in the extraction of tooth roots, and assist with gestures of this form:
And if there is any swelling, reduce it gradually, and keep it in place with the mouth so that it does not escape or spread until all the bone has been thoroughly treated; then restore the area with appropriate medicines.
But if there is a small hole in the bone, then remove that part of the decay and its blackness, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter XXXII.
On the treatment of prominent teeth over other teeth.
When teeth are prominent and do not follow their natural course because of their form being irregular, especially when this affects women, particularly those who are venerable; therefore, you should consider: if a tooth is too close to another tooth and there is no possibility of extraction or filling, then remove it; but make sure it is not attached to another tooth, so use this instrument whose shape resembles that of a pig's tusk, made of Indian steel with an extremely sharp point.
And let your incision be such that you incise it in too few days because of the hardness of the tooth, and may it not become infected by any other teeth.
And if it is a prominent tooth to be filed, then file that with an Indian iron file, which has the following form:
And let it be entirely of Indian iron and polish it accordingly; let it have small sharp points like the file on which needles are made, with which the tooth is filed little by little over several days with ease, so that no other tooth becomes infected and causes pain. And then smooth that at the end, and polish that with some razors.
And if it is a tooth of which part is broken and the tongue touches it, then you should file down the inside where the sharpness of that piece is, until it is smoothed and evened out, so that it is filled in such a way that the tongue does not injure itself nor the word be corrupted.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the restoration of infected teeth with gold or silver filling.
When it happens that the front teeth are loosened or displaced by impact or fall, and the patient cannot bite on what they eat, and you have treated them with styptic medicines, but your treatment has not been effective, then to prevent them from becoming infected, strengthen those that are loose with a filling of gold and not silver, for silver will tarnish, whereas gold will not change in any way.
And let the filling be in such subtlety and thickness according to the quantity that can fit between the teeth; and the method of applying it is that you take the filling and place twice as much of it between two healthy teeth, then increase the filling in two spiral forms between one or more infected teeth until it reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. From there proceed with the tissue into the part from which you began, and stretch your hand with ease and skill so that it is not harmed.
And hold the thread with which you stretch the roots of the teeth so that they do not escape.
And then cut the excess part of the thread using forceps or scissors, and attach it to each place with your fingers, and twist and hide them between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth, so that it does not injure the tongue. And then cut that as narrowly as a Roman would.
But if it becomes loose or broken, stretch that thread or those threads with another thread throughout the entire time.
And this is the form of the teeth and the figure of the correction of two healthy teeth and of two damaged teeth, as you see these tied:
And when it happens that one tooth or two are returned to their proper places upon setting, and they grow back as we have recommended, and they recover.
And only a skilled artisan and wise person can do this.
And sometimes a piece of cow bone is shaped for some reason in the form of a tooth, and it is placed in the location where the tooth is missing, and it is stretched as we have said, and it remains and serves there for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the incision of the membrane that is on the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament, which was mentioned above, either belongs to the tongue naturally from birth or has become accidental due to an illness that has already healed.
And the operation is when men open the mouth of the patient, and their head is positioned properly; then they lift the tongue and proceed to cut that nerve ligament longitudinally until the tongue is freed from its attachment.
If there is any hardness or lump in that area, which has become solid due to illness, then you gently cut it longitudinally until the ligament is divided and the lump is dissolved.
And be careful not to make a tear in the surface of the skin so that you do not cut the arteries and cause bleeding.
And then wash the remnants of the incision with fresh water or vinegar or salt water; and afterward place linen below the tongue. So the patient should hold a piece of cloth between their teeth each night to prevent the wound from healing too quickly.
But if bleeding occurs, place over the area vitriol powder; but if the blood continues to flow, then cauterize the location with a hot cautery iron that is appropriate for this. And then proceed with the treatment using other methods until it is healed.
Chapter XXXV.
On the Extraction of a Sublingual Cyst beneath the Tongue.
Occasionally, a cyst appears beneath the tongue, resembling a small swelling that prevents the tongue from its natural movement; and it can grow large enough to fill the mouth.
And in the operation you should place the patient sixty paces facing the sun and consider the abscess.
And if you see it of dark color or black and hard, which the patient does not feel, then do not touch that which is crunching.
And if it is inclined to whiteness, in which there is humidity, then you place the incision on it, and cut it with a fine scalpel, and cut it from all sides; and if blood flows during the time of your operation, then you pause above three times, which is vitriol, until the blood constricts.
And then return to your work once you have treated it properly, after washing it with vinegar and salt; and treat that with the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter XXXVI.
On the extraction of abscesses from both ears and those that occur in the throat and other abscesses.
When you encounter an abscess in the throat, which are glands that are somewhat assembled together, some of which appear outside and are called ears, when you treat them as we have said in the division and they do not heal, then consider if the abscess is hard, dark colored, with little sensation, then do not touch that with a metal instrument.
And if it is red in color and the root is thick, then do not touch that with a metal instrument due to the flow of blood; instead, let that mature, and then either you will remove it, or it will rupture on its own.
And if it is of white color, hard, and the layer beneath it is thin, then that is what should be incised.
And the operation on it is that you consider before your operation whether the abscess caused by heat has already healed or if there has been any reduction; and then have the patient sit facing the sunlight, and his head should be towards you, and open his mouth, and the assistant takes with their hands the tongue of him below with an instrument of noble form which has this shape:
And it should be made of silver or iron, and it should be thin like a knife.
Then when you press with that the tongue and the abscess is shown to you, look at it closely with your vision, then take some and place that in the cavity, and pull it out to the sides as much as you can. Or take something from the tunics to use as a cause, and then incise that with an instrument which has this shape:
Or take something from the tunics to use as a cause, and then incise that with an instrument which has this shape: and it resembles a pair of forceps, but the ends are curved, so that the opening of each is aligned with the other, sharp, made of iron.
But if this instrument is not available to you, then incise that with a scalpel which has this shape: It should be sharp on one side and not sharp on the other side.
And after you have incised one slit, incise the other in this same manner of incision, and then the patient should wash with fresh water.
If blood flows out, guard and use water from the decoction of thorns of mulberry or leaves of myrtle and similar to those of styptic causes until the flow of blood be restrained, and then cure it as it is healed.
And sometimes these pus discharges in the neck or other abscesses in those two ears, and incise them as it is said about the incision of two melons similarly.
And I had already treated a woman with an abscess which was born in her neck, the abscess tended to have a dark color and little feeling, it had already closed the passage of her throat, and the woman breathed very narrowly, and she had been prohibited from eating and drinking water, and she would have reached death if I had not acted for one day or two.
And the abscess had become so swollen that it had made two bulges from which it drained through two tracts.
Then I decided to surgically incise one of them and inserted my finger into it, and when pulled out, a large piece came with it, and then I incised the other tract from where I understood it originated, and then I closed its opening, and compressed its tongue, and then I placed the poultice on that abscess.
And then I removed a large part of it, and only a little blood came out from it. Then her throat was completely clear, and she began to drink water immediately, and soon after she started eating food. I did not need to incise that abscess again for a long time, but another abscess appeared in its place, which I incised before it could spread further.
Thus I cut and cauterized the tumor of his neck, thus completed the procedure; and then I walked around and did not know that God had done it all for me.
Chapter XXXVII.
On the incision of the shoulder when it is inflamed.
When there is a discharge from the shoulder, and it is inflamed and swollen, it is then called perpendicular; and if it is thick and round in the lower part, it is then called a bubba.
But when it is cured with what we have mentioned in the division and does not conform to your treatment, and you also encounter cases where an abscess has been cauterized but still persists and is subtle, then you should incise the rounded parts that are aggregated; and if it extends over a long length and is bloody, of dark color or black, and is not in the center, then you should attempt the incision because there is fear of malady in those areas.
And when you see it according to the method I have described regarding its whiteness and length, that you should recognize the malady and press the tongue of it with an instrument which reveals its form and then fix the incision in the new area, and pull it down and incise it with one of two instruments which are mentioned for the double incision.
And you should not incise more than what is appropriate according to the natural limit without exceeding it, for if you incise too much, you will harm the patient.
And after the incision, use what we have recommended for the double incision, and treat it until it is healed.
If the patient is frightened by the incision, use an instrument in the cauterization of him without fear, and the method of the cautery on him is nothing but sharp water, and that you place the head of the patient on your lap, and then press his tongue with the instrument which we have said, and then take the water which we have mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and confirm it with that cause alive, and place it not thick nor subtle, and with that fill the concavity of the instrument whose form is as follows:
Let the extremity of the cautery in which the medicine is placed be like the hollow of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the tongue, and the patient lies on his back so that saliva or mucus from the mouth of that person by the intrinsic medicine does not descend from any cause onto him and thus disturb him.
And then apply your hand with the medicine, and press that onto the uvula for a short while until you see it become blackened and numbed by the bite of the medicine.
And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the end of the cautery, and dip the cotton in the medicine, and insert the cautery with the cotton into a tube so that the cotton adheres to that tube, and do this as many times as needed until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the uvula, and then leave it alone until it dries and falls off after three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, do so, and after the cauterization, clean around the uvula with cotton dipped in syrup, and modify it with what is around it from the medicine, and wash it with fragrant water, and treat it externally with embrocation, and take care of it with remedies until it is healed.
And this kind of treatment is safer than incision and less painful from fear.
In some cases, the uvula is treated with something more subtle than cauterization and incision. It is cured by sub-fumigation in this manner: take a poultice, hyssop, origany, rue, Arabian saffron, chamomile, and similar herbs; adjust all these causes or some of them in a pot, and steep them in fresh water which has been boiled, and let it boil; the water should be moderately thickened with flour, and place a trough at the center of the pot, on top of which place an instrument shaped like a sieve made of straw in this manner: it can be made of silver or straw.
And insert the extremity into the mouth of the patient so that the vapor reaches the uvula, until the uvula is sufficiently affected.
And repeat this procedure several times until it is dry, and be careful not to perform this treatment at the beginning of the onset of abscesses, as it would overly affect the abscess. You should not do this; instead, you should wait for the abscess to declare itself before cauterizing it.
But if this instrument is not present, take a cane and attach a hollow tube of horn to one end, so that the mouth of the patient is covered, as the tube of horn prevents the vapor from entering the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter XXXVIII.
On the extraction of spines and what adheres to the skin and other causes.
Move gently around the belly or spine of the patient or for some other reason.
Then look to remove what is apparent there where your sight rests, and then press your tongue or use an instrument in the sunlight so that it may be revealed what is in the throat.
And what does not appear and is hidden in the throat, you should make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, because a cause attached to the throat will be easily expelled by vomiting; or have the patient swallow a piece of bread or a leaf of lettuce or a dry biscuit; or take a piece of dried sea sponge and tie it firmly with thread, and then have the patient swallow that, as when it reaches the place where there are thorns, then it will carry them out. And take an eel and bind it with thread, then place it inside and gradually extract it; or take a probe and gradually insert and withdraw it.
And if it is not expelled by the methods we have mentioned, then use an instrument made of lead in this form:
And it should be as thick as a small finger, and at the end of it there should be a hook that you can easily insert into the patient's throat.
And have the patient's head raised upwards, and be careful not to touch the bone of the throat, so that it does not injure the patient or cause pain in the bones or spine, or make the patient afraid.
However, if the patient himself inserts it, it is better, due to his knowledge of the location of the attached cause and to avoid pain, and he should pull his hand with the instrument upwards, all this according to the quantity prepared within him so that it may be expelled.
Chapter XXXIX.
On the Removal of a Leech Attached to the Throat.
When treating a leech with the water we have mentioned in the section on treatment using medicines and not compresses, consider the time of day regarding the position of the sun in relation to the patient's throat, since you have already swollen the tongue of the patient with the instrument that we recommended.
Then if you see the leech, remove it gently with your fingertips or with a small instrument carefully.
And if it is not possible for you to remove it with your hands, then take a concave tube and insert it into the patient's throat, and immediately place a heated iron in the concavity of the tube, and do this as many times as necessary (until the leech dies and falls off), and keep the patient from water for the entire day.
And then take a container filled with clean water and open the patient's mouth to it, and wash the mouth with it; and do not allow any other substance to be swallowed and change the water every hour or so because as soon as the leech feels the water it will cling.
But if it is not possible to follow the method we have recommended, then smoke the patient with cyprus or asafetida using the instrument we recommended for smoking the uvula.
Do this, and see what happens.
And there is another method of operation in smoking the leech, which is that you take an olive shell in which there are ignited coals and the shell is covered with a cloth in the middle of which there is a hole, and attach the end of the instrument to this hole; then perform the smoke infusion from above; and place the patient's mouth on the end of the tube, and close their mouth so that the smoke does not escape until you know that the smoke has reached the leech, for the leech will fall off immediately.
And if it does not cough, then at this time repeat the subincision, and prevent the patient from drinking, and let them eat salty and dry foods, and let them not drink water, and thus it is necessary that this regimen be avoided.
And this is the form of the instrument with which blood from the gum is sucked when it is visible; and it resembles forceps as well, but there is some curvature in it. which is placed in the gum, and the tip of it resembles the beak of a heron, which is made rough with a file, so that when it presses against something, it does not slip.
Chapter 40.
In which is the summary of the speech on the preparation for abscesses and their incision.
Abscesses are of various kinds and methods, as will be recalled for each one in this book.
And they vary in their puncture and incision by two methods, one of which is according to the nature of the abscess itself; and due to what it compresses of humidity.
And the second method is according to the locations in which they occur on the body, for an abscess occurring in the head is different from one occurring in the buttocks; and an abscess occurring in a fleshy area is different from one occurring at a joint; and each has its treatment by operation.
And there are some abscesses that should not be punctured but only when the pus has properly matured or at their completion.
So abscesses that must be drained when they are crosswise [across joints], and have not matured according to their proper time, just as abscesses that are near the joints, for an abscess when it is close to a joint extends its nature within so that what is around it becomes corrupt, and weakens the tendons or nerves of that joint, thereby causing contraction of that limb; or if it is near a principal member, and you delay perforating it until it has matured in the principal member; or if it is near the anus, therefore pierce it before it has matured; but do not pierce it raw when it is not fully digested, except for one that is near the anus, so that pus does not escape from it and spread to the inner parts of the anus, thus forming a fistula, or that it be made in its place where it cannot heal. It is necessary that you know the time for piercing the abscess, and this is when it has matured.
And you should know that it is mature when there is relief from pain and the cessation of fever, and also with the reduction in hardness and pulse, and the sharpness of the abscess head, and other signs.
And you must know that the puncture should be made in the place where the pus is lowest in the abscess and from where pus will come for the healing of the elevation.
And the puncture should always be made along the length of the flesh or body if the abscesses are in the parts of the back or legs, and in places of tendons, ligaments, nerves; and finally, in all similar places where there is no reflection; for in those that reflect, use puncture according to that member.
When the abscess is in a fleshy area, it is better to postpone the incision until the second stage of digestion is confirmed, as we have said; for if you pierce it before that, it will prolong the suffering of the patient and increase foul matter, and make hard the lips of the wound and deepen it.
However, some abscesses should be pierced according to the thickness of the flesh or part of the body due to necessity, or as is appropriate for the limb in question.
And it is necessary to administer a small incision in small abscesses, and in large abscesses, a larger or wider incision according to the variety of their size.
And in other cases of abscesses where it is necessary to hollow out and cut deeply, when this is done as needed, such as when it reaches a stage that requires it, just as occurs with excessive dubec (inguinal swellings) and buboes.
And of those that are incised with a section having three corners; and of those in which it is incised like the shape of a laurel leaf, as in the abscesses of the armpits.
And in those where a round incision or a crescent-shaped incision is administered, similar to those cases.
However, for those whose abscesses are superficial like lions' (likely a metaphor or specific condition), it is necessary to pierce them with a shallow incision or simply a flat one.
And when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, you should pierce it but do not hesitate to extract all the pus at that time; having extracted a portion from it, on another day open the abscess inside, and then extract a larger portion, and repeat this multiple times according to gradation until it is fully drained.
If the patient is a young male, or a woman in her period, or an infant, or an old and infirm person, for the animal spirit quickly dissolves with the evacuation of the humor, the sick person easily dies, therefore you should operate.
Keep this chapter with great caution, and when you make the incision in which you puncture these abscesses, make sure to clean the pus and then consider whether the pus of the abscess is small, or if it is a flat section; if so, administer some of it or use antiseptic cotton. And if the abscess is large, and the wounds from the incision are many, then place wax in each part until they heal.
And if in the abscess a part of the cord is cut or you have hollowed it out in the circuit, then you should fill it with old cloth or thread made from linen, as when you treat ulcers or wounds without moisture; and strengthen that by the third day; and thereafter proceed to care for it using whatever remedies are necessary until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs during the time of your operation, then administer cold water and vinegar in which you soak a piece of linen, and place that on the site of the bleeding quickly.
But if the bleeding persists, then you should administer treatment and care and use powders that have been recommended in various places of this our book and its divisions.
But if it is in the winter season, and the location of the abscess is near many nerves, then you should mix powders with vinegar and heated oil, and place them on those locations.
And if it is in summer, and the places are fleshy, you should apply poultices soaked in water and oil or in wine and oil, and all of this should be rubbed until the third day, as we have said, and then you should open up the abscess and clean that area and proceed with the treatment of it with what is suitable for it until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally, but according to the method of distinction I have already said about every abscess in what manner is the way of treating it separately by chapters.
Chapter XLI.
On the Incision of Abscesses That Occur in the Neck Area.
In the neck area, small abscesses occur, and they are of various types of nodes that contain membranes similar to their structure; and the types vary in their characteristics.
And they are soft to the touch, and those that contain moisture similar to jelly. And those that contain moisture similar to dust, and resemble them; and those that are like stones, hard.
And in the incision of all of them, and in the extraction of those, there is no fear, as the extraction of those does not harm the arteries.
And the procedure in incising those is that you should press them with an instrument whose shape is similar to this, which is called an entrometer, until you know what it contains.
For if what it contains is moisture, then you should cut that with an incision according to its length with a superficial incision according to this form:
And begin at the section of the line of B and enter into the line of G. Then when the moisture is evacuated, you shall excise that skin which contains that moisture, and do not leave any part of it remaining, for if left behind it will return as a source of trouble.
And then immerse the wound in Egyptian oil if it is available, and if not, in salt water; and fill that with the dressing and leave it there for another day, for in this way you will dissolve all that remains of that skin layer.
And then repeat the treatment on that wound with Egyptian oil a second and third time if necessary, until you are confident that there is no remaining cause of that skin layer; now care for the wound with the dressing until it is healed.
And if an abscess contains hard nodes, then you should incise it according to this method: in a section having the shape of a cross, and place incisions between both lips of the section and scrape it thoroughly from all sides, and exert your skill in extracting the membrane that contains it.
But if your experience opposes this, then do as I have described initially.
And similarly, treat the calcified abscess with sectioning and healing as we have told you; and cutting into a calcified abscess is easier, because it contains little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes in some of these abscesses which occur on the head in people who are not very moist; this cause is because I once incised an abscess on the head of an old woman and found within it a hard, rough, white stone-like mass that could not be removed, and if touched by anyone with percussion, it would cause pain.
In other abscesses that occur in the head, this is not the chapter for them; likewise, the abscesses that appear on children's heads and behind the ears should not be included here. However, the section of all these shall be a superficial section.
And pause the puncturing of those from the lower parts so that the contents of the abscess are discharged to the lower parts.
And treat those accordingly as is appropriate for that condition.
Chapter XLII.
On the section about treating scrofula, which occurs in the neck.
Often these abscesses occur in the neck and behind the ears; and at times they appear in other parts of the body.
And those that occur in the neck often arise between the layers of tissue there, and every scrofula among them is within its own proper membrane, just as in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have described.
And the types of these scrofulas are various; some are lithic, some contain moisture, and among them are malignant ones that do not respond to treatment.
Therefore, when you find one that is well disposed upon examination, whose color matches the skin tone and which does not adhere to the neck's nerves or veins, and is not hard, it is appropriate to incise it with superficial cuts above and below the body of the lesion along the line G between the line B, and peel it off completely from all sides. Extend two flaps of the skin an inch or more if necessary, as we have described for abscesses of the head, and extract it bit by bit.
It is necessary to be careful that the vein or nerve is not cut.
And let the scraping not be sharp, so that it does not irritate your hand during the incision or disturb the patient; and do not cut what is not necessary to cut. For if you should cut a vein or artery and stop bleeding from it, then apply green vitriol powder or other powders that cause blood flow, and bind the wound tightly; let the pus remain inside where the inflammation subsides, so that the swelling becomes softened and begins to rot. When the bleeding has stopped.
Then return to your operation until you have completed it.
And then ask the patient if there is any cause for other small swellings, and if so, incise those and clean them.
And if there is a large vein at the base of the swelling, be careful not to incise that swelling from its base. Also be careful to apply firm bandages and let that remain until it heals on its own without harm.
And then fill the wound with old lint that you have already soaked in Egyptian oil, and then dress it.
And if you incise the swelling completely, then be sure to approximate the two edges of the wound and ensure that pus comes out so that no excess fluid remains.
But if you see that there is excess fluid due to the size of the swelling, then be sure to incise the base of it, drain it, and apply sutures as described.
And for those scrofulas that contain humidity, make an incision at the surface where it appears to you at the site of its maturation, and do not pierce down to the pus deep within the body, as we have said.
And around the area of the incision, apply treatments and remedies appropriate for such cases, so that it corrodes what remains of the corruption. And when you see the wound already cleansed, now apply remedies to generate new flesh until it is healed.
Chapter XLIII.
On the incision of the pustule of an accidental abscess in the groin.
The ancients called this incision an epipustule, and I have not seen anyone in our land perform it in this manner.
And this is the inscription of the speech of those [ancients].
But in men who have scrofula, it is advisable to avoid the extraction of the epipustule, as it is not beneficial because all the veins and major channels are affected here.
But in those who have boil-like swellings in the mouth, groin, or ears, when the illness is not in the bone, it is advisable to use incision of the epipustule to avoid the damage that is caused by perforation.
Therefore, when we want to do this, incise the epipustule below three circles of the epipustule or four small sections in width between two circles in quantity. The incision should be made in the flesh, not in the cartilage, and this location is suitable for incision because it lacks flesh and the blood vessels there are prominent.
And if the surgeon is cautious, he should wait until the vein expands due to blood congestion and then cut the vein so as to reach the channel, but avoid cutting into the vessels that carry blood if you see any reason not to.
And then incise the cicatrix as we have recommended; and this is significant regarding the section of the channel by the flesh which is around that which is disrupted there, and by the separation of the voice.
And leave the wound untreated for some time.
Then when the time comes for which that perforation is feared, add two lips of the edge of the wound, and close it up except where the cartilage is.
And then administer medicines that cause flesh to grow until it is healed.
And Al-Bucasis says that the essence of what we have recommended is to see whether some of these abscesses have already obstructed the vein of the patient, and the patient has come close to death, and their breath is cut off, and some would incise the epiglottis so that the patient can breathe over the site of the wound for some respiration, and thus be saved from death.
And by this command, leave the wound untreated until the malignity of the disease is consumed; and the malignity lasts for three days; therefore, at that time, treat the wound accordingly until it is healed.
And what I have seen with my own eyes is that a servant took a knife and cut it into her own vein, cutting off a part of the windpipe's cartilage, and I was called to treat her. I found the wound as if it were that of a dying person, and uncovered the wound to see the blood that had come out of it in small amounts.
Thus he confessed that he had not incised any vein or ligature, and the wind issued from the ulcer, and cured her inside as if by fire.
And it did not affect the servant unless he coughed in his voice, and after some days he returned to the condition of his disposition.
And now I say that in the section of the epiploon there is no fear.
Chapter XLIV.
Of the section on abscess which occurs in the groin outside, and it is called hernia of the groin.
This abscess called hernia of the groin is a large abscess according to the color of the skin, and it often occurs in women.
And it happens in two ways, either naturally or accidentally.
And in the natural case, there is no need for treatment.
And the accidental case happens in two ways, one of which is similar to fatty lumps, and the other way is similar to an abscess caused by arterial obstruction, and in that case there is cause for concern. And it does not require that pressure be applied with force except in the case where it is small. And you should proceed with caution and investigate it carefully. If you find it similar to a fatty lump and it is not attached to any veins, then incise it as you would a lymph node and extract what contains the fluid inside; otherwise, thoroughly examine its nature, and afterwards treat the area as necessary until it is healed.
Chapter XLV.
On the division of types of knots.
Of the knots there are various types, and I have already mentioned all their types in the division.
And it is necessary for me to recount thus the difference between a knot and an ulcer as it causes doubt.
Therefore, I say that an ulcer is a condition of heat and fever and intense pain within, where the ebullition of the excess is settled; and it completes putrefaction when then the fever, the pain, and the heat cease.
But a knot is not a condition of heat nor fever nor pain, and it resembles that swelling which occurs in the private parts.
And it is according to the color of the skin, and the beginning of it is like a pimple and it forms in various ways, whether larger or smaller, and they are of two types, either dry or fatty and contain moisture, and the colors of the moisture are various, as I have said in the division.
Therefore, when you come to the treatment of a knot, you should express it and inquire about its origin at the beginning, which is called an intrusion, as its form will be described in the chapter that follows this one.
And this is the method of inquiry for abscesses and all knots, which is to take this origin and insert it into the most moist part that you find in the abscess, and you will rotate it little by little until you know that the origin has already penetrated the tissue.
And subsequently, according to the size of the abscess, withdraw your hand, and then extract the insertion and observe what has come out in its wake.
And if the flowing moisture, of whatever color it may be, is disregarded, then at that time you incise it with a superficial section as I have said regarding other abscesses.
And if from the remains of the introitus you do not have moisture, then you will know that it is greasy.
And if it is dry to the touch with a superficial section having the form of a cross according to this shape: As I have taught you, suspend it in this manner, and scrape the cord from all sides with ease, and keep the skin or sac if you can over that area, and extract that pus at the knot.
But if it is ruptured during the operation, it is not possible to extract that pus, and you should frequently apply dressings for this reason, and then you will extract it in stages until none of it remains due to any cause.
For if some of it remains due to any cause, little or much, the knot will return as is worse.
And if you see that some of it remains in small parts, then complete the treatment with certain corrosive and sharp powders and strengthen the area, and place over that which is an affected abscess a dressing, and treat that from another form of treatment until it is healed.
And if the knot is large, then you cut both sides of it and treat that with what causes healing.
But if a pulsating or non-pulsating vein is against it, then you should cauterize and fill the place three times with vitriol and leave that open for two days or three until the dressing has become loose and the boiling of the blood subsides; and afterwards return to incising what remains of the knot.

Chapter XLVII.
On the treatment of men's nipples, which are similar to those of women.

Chapter XLVIII.
On the Incision of Abscesses That Are Located in the Throat.
These abscesses that occur in the throat are among the types of scrofula, hard swellings, which contain a core that can be extracted.
And only some of those contain moisture; those that do contain moisture should be incised in a crescent shape as shown in this figure: from the line G to the line B. Once all the pus inside the abscess has been evacuated, then fill it with boiled cloth and leave it for another day.

Chapter XLIX.
On the Incision of Abscesses That Occur Due to Artery or Vein.
When an artery is ruptured and the area around it becomes inflamed, an abscess may occur. The signs by which one can determine whether inflammation and an abscess are due to an artery or a vein: when the abscess is due to an artery, it tends to be elongated near the front of the body; and if you press on the abscess with your fingers, you will hear a hissing sound.
And that which is from a vein is round on the surface of the body.
And the section on this abscess is incorrect, especially in those areas where there are joints, and in the armpits, and in the neck, and in many parts of the body; it is serious, therefore it should be avoided that its treatment involves iron, and those that are in the extremities or on the head, it should be avoided.
For that which is due to inflammation of the artery opening, divide it in the longest section; and then cut with hooks near the section and near the artery, and remove the tunics until it is exposed.
And then place on that needle and make it penetrate into the other side, and tie the artery with a double knot with a needle in two places, — which I explain in the extraction of two arteries which are in the thighs.
And then press the place which is between two ligaments with a probe until the blood comes out completely, and the abscess dissolves.
And then administer treatment in which pus is produced until the ligaments fall off; and then treat it with appropriate ointments so that it may heal.
And if it is an abscess due to the section of a vein, now you should hold with your hand what is possible of the abscess in its longest part, and then insert the needle below the place where you have held with your hand, and make it penetrate; and tie it double at that point so that you can extract it from the other side, and then ligate the abscess with a strong thread as I have told you regarding the grapevine thread on both sides of the middle of the filament.
If you fear that you will extract the thread, then place a needle with wax under the abscess; first insert the tip of the needle and strengthen its wax in four places.
When the abscess is dry in its middle part and when what is inside it has come out, then cut away the excess skin, and leave what is adhered to it.
And then apply dust which you have submerged in wine and oil, and subsequently administer the treatment made with cords and bandages until it is healed.
Chapter L of the abscess which occurs due to the twisting of nerves.
Just as an abscess can occur in arteries and veins, similarly, an abscess occurs in nerves when they are struck or due to excessive labor, and likewise.
And it is done with the hand in the places of the helpers or at the heel, or in any place where the joints are usually troubled.
And it is a severe abscess, and its color resembles that of the surrounding flesh. It is in one of its major dispositions when it is tight with inflammation, and when the patient appears dazed.
And it is not an abscess aggregated at the front of the body, and therefore it is below the skin, and it affects all parts equally and does not go to the parts in front or behind.
Therefore, when it is in the joints, there is no need for it to be enclosed with iron, as it would easily lead to contraction.
Make a cut in the head or forehead with a spatum. If it is a small abscess, open it with forceps and cut it out with razitz.
And if it is large, then suspend it with an incision, and scrape that area, and afterwards close it; and stitch the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
On the incision of an abscess that occurs on the cheek as it occurs on the abdomen.
Indeed, for some people there appear growths on their abdomens and other parts of their bodies, which are called fungous because they resemble mushrooms, whose root is fine and the head of it is large; and often the lips of these are smooth, and among them are those that are small, and also those that are magnificently enlarged.
And I myself have seen one in whose abdomen there were two growths that resembled mushrooms, and there was no difference between those white ones, with fine roots.
And the lips of those growths were inverted and split, and moisture continually flowed from them.
Therefore, I incised them and found inside one weighing about eighteen ounces and the other about ten ounces.
And the procedure in incising them is that you should consider whether the malady is humid, and if the color of the growth is white, humid, with a fine root; then you should incise it with a broad spatum, and immediately over a flame cauterize it, because when dealing with such growths, the section of it expels much blood; and if there is bleeding, cauterize that area.
And if the wound is small and fearful, guard against cutting with iron; instead take fresh lead foil, and apply it to that part in the manner we have described; and leave it for two days.
And so adjust it in the tightness of the lead thread; therefore, do not stop applying more lead as needed until it is separated and falls off on its own.
But if the part is of thick substance, then in cutting it there is some cause for fear and dread, especially in that which is in the abdomen.
But it is possible that you might cut half of it, and then cauterize that part so that it does not return.
And then treat it until the wound is healed.
And be careful not to presume to cut a part that is dark in color, has little sensation, and is easily movable, for such a part is likely cancerous.
And now I will turn to recall something related to this.
Chapter LIIL
On the treatment of swelling of the navel.
Swelling of the navel is caused by various reasons; it can be due to fissures in the abdomen that are above the belly, and in such cases, linen or bladder tissue is used depending on other ruptures; or it can be from a wound where blood flows from a vein or artery, depending on the situation, or due to trapped air.
But if it is due to a fissure in the abdomen and the protrusion of tissue, then the color of the swelling is similar to the color of the body; it is painless, and subsequently appears in various situations. And if it is by the exit from the bowel, then its situation is in that place which we have recounted, but with much greater difference, and when you press that area with your hand, it immediately retreats; and it is very light there, and is magnified by means of bath and vigorous labor.
And if it is due to moisture, then it is a lion and is not hidden when you press with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor is it hard.
And if it is due to blood, then with these signs the abscess appears blackish and declining.
And if it is due to excess flesh, then it is a severe abscess, large and hard, and it is embedded in proportion to its quantity.
And if it is due to gas, then the touch of it is like a lion.
And the operation in this case is that you must consider whether the prominence of the navel is due to blood from an artery, or a vein, or gas; now it behooves you to be cautious about treating it, for there is fear and dread as I have taught you in the chapter where I have spoken about abscesses from arteries and veins.
And if the prominence of the navel is due to the bowel or the membrane, then it behooves you to order the patient to lie on their back and extend themselves; then examine around the entire navel with your hand, and then order them to stand between your hands on their back; then encircle with a bandage wide in the circumference of the navel where you have examined with your hand; then extend half of the abscess above an inch or so; then tie it around the circumference firmly with waxed thread or linen in a proper manner.
From the side of the swelling of the abscess extended over the bandage, place your finger in that spot and search for the purse; and if you have found it or guarded it by the bandage, now extract it immediately and push the purse to the parts inside the belly.
And if you have found the membrane, now take that one-inch scalpel; and cut into the excess tissues of it.
But if it is contrary to your artery or vein, now encircle that spot, and then return to your operation; take two needles and place them in those two strong points and insert the two needles around the circumference you have made around the abscess in a crisscross manner, which should be penetrated; and then press the abscess into four parts over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and let the place inside where the extended flesh will drain and fall off by itself; or cut it when it is softened and will drain, and then treat that area with water from the decoctions until it is healed.
And if the prominence of the navel is of flesh or due to humidity, now it is necessary that you conquer the abscess as we have said and extract the blood or moisture that you find there, and then treat that area with what is needed to heal and strengthen the place.
Chapter LIII.
On the Treatment of Cancer.
We have already said, in the division, about a type of cancer and in what manner it is treated with medicines, and that it is difficult to cure without surgery because it cannot heal on its own; we also mentioned cancer arising in the major parts and the caution required for its treatment.
And let them say the ancients that in some cases cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible, just as with the cranc which is in the mammary glands or in the armpit and similar parts assigned to remove all of it, especially when it begins small.
But when it is old and hard, do not try to deal with it yourself, for I cannot heal anyone from that, nor have I seen another who could, nor has anyone reached such a condition.
And the operation in it is when it is appropriate, as we have said, that you precede and leave the patient to purge from black bile for some days, and then let them bleed if there is obvious retention.
And then prepare the patient with preparation in which the operation is located, and then place hooks in the wound that are necessary for it; and then cauterize from all sides with a well-sewn leather bag to ensure thoroughness so that no cause remains for its recurrence. And let the blood flow, and do not apply abscesses hastily to it, and press the areas, and wipe away the thick blood with your hand or with an instrument that is suitable among the available tools.
But if it is contrary to let the thick blood flow and you must cut into a vein or artery, then cauterize that area until the flow of blood stops, and afterward treat it with another method until it is healed.
Chapter 46.

On the swelling that occurs in the foreskin at the head of the penis, and on corruption, blackening, and adherence of the foreskin to the head of the penis.

Chapter LVII.
On the purification of children and the treatment of what occurs during circumcision.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity like other diseases; but since it is part of our procedure and our will, and because it primarily affects children, it is necessary that we establish the best method and the most gentle way to ensure their health.
Therefore, I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books, as they do not use it in their laws. The reason for this is that we have acquired through experience. What I have found is that most surgeons and barbers use a scalpel and forceps for circumcision and purification; they use bandages made of thread and perform incisions with the fingernail.
And thus I have described all these methods and did not discover a better modification with scissors, with thread as in the modification with the nail, because most times you avoid the cicatrix above, because the cicatrix of the foreskin has two tunics, and remove the tunic below, and it is necessary for another incision and certain pain. And in the modification which is done with alphabetization, which uses the fingers, as barbers do, the incision of the extremity of the male member is not secured, because it easily enters into the groove.
But in the operation with the nail, you easily forget and ruin your operation. And the cicatrix of the child is short by nature, and many times they are in such a way that there is no need for modification, and I have seen that.
But concerning the modification that is done with scissors and thread with precision, the goodness, experience reveals to me, since the scissors should be of proportional incision, because the sharpness above is also like the sharpness below; and thus when you press your hand according to the proportions of both sharpnesses, and you make an incision following a comparison at once; and it is done with compression of the thread in a figure containing the cicatrix of the penis from all sides, with which you catch no error whatsoever.
And the method of the first operation is that you make the child lie down if he is one whom I understand to be small, so that you only tie the thread around his penis and leave it for another day.
And then make him happy in every way that is possible to you according to what his intelligence can receive. Let him be brought to you already prepared and not in a state of fear; and hide the scissors in your hand or put them out of sight, so that the child does not see them or any other instrument.
Then place your hand on his chin and blow into the ear, and insert it until the tip of the forceps comes out, and then remove what has adhered to it from impurities.
The shape of the scissors for removal is as follows:
Let one set of handles be unsharpened and the other sharpened, so that the tip of the clamp does not come out, and let the length of both blades be equal to the length of two handbreadths alike.
But the mistake in removal is that you may flee too far from the inner ear entirely, or part of it due to the incision.
Therefore, you should extend that from your hour to your nail in front of the swelling of the area and incise it according to equality.
And if you cannot hold it with your finger, then extract it with a probe and incise it.
And if three days have passed and the swelling remains below the testicle, then you should leave what is inside until the abscess bursts on its own, and when it has discharged, clean that area well, and incise it as soon as you are prepared.
And be careful of the head of the testicle, for it should not suffer from this.
And thus, treat that area with the powders that the plague consolidates from those we have recommended in the chapter on powders.
And if it is incised too much from the tendon, and you follow the instructions above, this will not harm it.
And treat that area with what we have said until it is healed.

If this is not done in this order, but rather follows a winding path.

Chapter 60.
On the Extraction of Stones.
We have already mentioned in the division the kinds of stones and their treatment with medicines, and the difference between stones generated in the kidneys and those generated in the bladder, as well as the signs of each.
And we have described the stone in which this operation is performed, and its section.
And the stone generated in the bladder properly is that which is lodged in the bladder.
And he who narrates it thus presents it clearly and concisely.
Therefore, I say that the stone generated in the bladder mostly affects children; and of the signs of it is that the urine which is emitted from the bladder resembles water in its subtlety, and there appears something like sand; and the patient scratches his penis, and small drops come out, and soon he is relieved; and with this you are given advice in words of those.
And it is easier to treat children with that condition until they have reached fourteen years; and treatment is difficult for the elderly. But in young people it is moderate compared to those extremes.
And for the one in whom the stone is larger and the treatment is longer; And if it is small, it is contrary to that.
Therefore, how we reach the treatment of that condition is to begin with cleansing the patient's bladder by extracting all the sediment that is in his urine; for that sediment sometimes prevents the passage of the stone due to its investigation.
And then the patient should lie on his back and be encouraged to drink plenty of water so that the stone descends to the neck of the bladder or jumps from its place, sometimes.
And then place the patient between the thighs properly prepared, and let him keep the things of it away, so that the bladder is made to incline towards the parts behind.
And there I examined that and all around it.
So if you feel the stone in the space, now you should cut it out immediately.
And if you do not catch it right away with your touch, now you should anoint your index finger of the left hand, if a female, or the middle finger if male and older, with oil, and insert it into their anus. And examine the stone until you feel it with your finger. And gradually turn it little by little into the bladder neck.
And then press on it with your finger, and expel it outwards towards the place where the section was made.
And command the minister to press the bladder with his hand; and command another minister to extend the thighs outward with one hand, and with the other hand to pull back the skin that is below the thighs, near the place where the section was made.
And then insert a spatula of this shape into it: and cut what is between the anus and the thighs, not in the middle, but more to the left side; and make the incision over the stone and have your finger in the anus, and make a transverse incision so that the external part of the incision is wide and the internal part narrow according to how much you can extract of the stone, and no more, because your finger in the anus presses the stone against it without difficulty after the incision.
And know that there are some stones which have angles and margins, because of which it is difficult to remove them surgically.
And that one is similar in appearance to a hazelnut, it is round, and it can be easily removed surgically.
Therefore, for that one which has angles and margins, adjust it into the fissure.
And if it is not removed easily, now you should apply something over it, or use a properly sized tool, the end of which is like a file with which you can work on the stone without avoiding it; or insert an intricate curved instrument below it.
But if you cannot do that, now slightly enlarge the wound, and if you see blood flowing, cauterize it with a tool dipped in tripedal vitriol.
But if there is more than one stone, at the beginning you press your hand to the mouth of the bladder, and then incise it slightly; and subsequently press gently nearby; and similarly proceed if they are in greater number than two.
And if the stone is very large, then it is ignorance to incise it with a large section, because this causes one of two bad outcomes: either death or that the patient suffers from continuous urine dripping, because the area does not heal completely.
But administer an expulsion so that it is removed, or devise a fracture with forceps to extract the stone in pieces.
Therefore, when you have completed your operation, now fill the wound with powder of aloes and dragon's blood, and cauterize it. And apply over it a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or in rose oil and water mixed, so that the abscess may more readily yield.
And let the patient lie on their side with the affected part uppermost, and do not loosen the bandage before the third day.
And when it has been unwrapped, anoint the area with water and a little oil, and then treat it with a decoction of pennyroyal and calamus until it is healed.
But if an abscess should occur in the wound, which is hard and adherent, and appears as though blood is congealed within it (and this is known by the prohibition of urination), then insert your finger into the wound and extract that blood, for if left untreated it will cause corruption and putrefaction of the bladder.
And then wash the wound with sour wine, water and salt, and apply whatever is appropriate to its condition until it is healed.
And it is necessary throughout the entire treatment that you examine both the wounds and adjust the medicines which are applied to the area.
And if the stone is small and is in the urethra, and is lodged there, and urination is prohibited, then treat it with what I recommend before resorting to section, for many have been accused of unnecessary section due to this treatment; and I say this, which is that you take a blunt iron probe, which has the following shape:
with a triangular end, sharp, attached to a handle.
Take some wax, and tie the thread beneath the stone with it so that it does not return to the bladder.
Insert the needle into the thread and pierce with ease until it reaches the stone. Rotate the extractor with your hand on that stone little by little, and as you enlarge the hole, allow the other end to penetrate; for the urine will immediately be relieved.
Then press your hand on what remains of the stone that is outside the thread, for it will break and be expelled with the urine, and the illness will be cured.
But if this treatment is not suitable for you due to some reason that prevents it, then tie a thread beneath the stone and another thread above the stone. Then cut into the stone on that thread between the two ties. Then extract it. Then untie the thread and clean the accumulated blood, which has formed around the wound.
It is not necessary to tie a thread below the stone except to prevent it from returning to the bladder, and the other thread above it, because when the thread near the stone is untied, the pain will return to its original location and the wound will become inflamed again.
And for this reason, it is necessary when tying the thread above to lift the pain to the upper parts or return to your initial care, as we have said, so that the wound does not become inflamed again.
Chapter LXI.
On the Removal of Stones from Women.
Stones in women are rarely formed.
If a stone is found in one of them, the treatment is difficult at that time, and it is prohibited by many methods.
One of them is that the woman is too weak.
And the second is that you do not find a woman who is sufficiently honest to reveal whether she has given it to the doctor when she is chaste, or whether she is one of those women who have husbands.
The third is that you do not find a woman who knows this art, especially the operation well.
And the fourth is that the site for the incision to remove a stone in women is far from the location of the stone, so it requires a distant incision, and this is dangerous.
However, if necessity compels you to do this, then it is necessary for you to take a trustworthy and knowledgeable woman, but few are found.
However, if you lack such a woman, then seek a discreet and careful man, or present a reputable midwife who is well-known for women's matters, or a woman who is proficient in this art.
Therefore, bring that person present and command them to do everything you would instruct, from the examination of the stone at the beginning; the reason for this is to ensure that the woman is honest, so they should place their finger in her and feel for the stone.
Then if you find that one, cut that below the joint, and now order him to incise upon that one.
But if it is not soft, now order the midwife to insert the joint into the woman’s vagina and feel for the stone, since she places her left hand on the bladder, and compress that one well.
Then if you find that one, now proceed to gradually remove it from the bladder's opening to the sides below as far as its root within until it reaches the base of the matter.
And then incise upon that point in the middle of the vagina where the base of the matter is, wherever it may be to him convenient and place the stone there, and let the said joint not be cut from the stone below it.
And let the incision be small at first; then insert the probe into that incision, and if you feel the stone, now adjust the incision according to the quantity that you know will allow the stone to be expelled from there.
And know that the types of stones are varied: some are small and hard, others are rough and sharp, long and round, and having edges.
Then you should know the types of stones for what you signify regarding what you want.
And if you wish to cause bleeding, apply vitriol thrice to that area for one hour until the blood is drawn, and then return to your operation until the stone is expelled.
Prepare those instruments which are mentioned in the extraction of stones from men, so that you may be assisted by them in your operation.
And if you see bleeding, and know that the expulsion of the blood is from the artery that is incised, then sprinkle powder on the area and stop it with a bandage and compression, and do not repeat this; and leave the stone there and do not extract it, for the patient will likely die. And then treat the wound.
When the sharpness of the bleeding has subsided after some days and the area has become clean, then return to your operation until the stone is dislodged.
Chapter LXII.
On the incision for an inguinal hernia.
An inguinal hernia is nothing but an accumulation of moisture in the white tissue beneath the tendon of the groin muscles, which contains the spermatic cord, and this is called a pouch.
And in some cases, it is in the proper fatty layer that nature has prepared for the passage of the spermatic cord, such that one may think it is another cord; and the tendon of the groin muscle is between this white pouch that we have mentioned, and this is not so.
The generation of this hernia is due to weakness that occurs in the groin muscles, causing this matter to protrude into them; and sometimes it happens due to impact on the groin area.
And this moisture can have various colors, for its color may incline to yellowish or reddish, or brown, or be black and thick, or clear and white, and the latter is better.
Here are the signs by which it is known where this accumulation of water is such as this: for if it is in the white skin we have mentioned, then at that moment the abscess is round and leaning slightly in length like a hen's egg, and the cheek appears, because the moisture extends on all sides.
And if the moisture is in the proper subcutaneous tissue, then the abscess is round but divided like a bird's egg, and thus it is estimated to be another kind of egg.
And if the moisture is between the cheekbone and the white skin, then feel below the center.
And when you want to know the color of the moisture, then examine the color of the moisture in the abscess through a probe of which the shape is known; and according to what appears in the marks left by the probe, judge what is inside.
When we come to the treatment of it with iron, then it is necessary to command the patient to be bloodletted if that is possible for you, and apply the body of the instrument to it.
And let it be raised up on his back on something slightly elevated, and place below it some dry cloths.
And you sit on the left side of him, and command the assistant to stand on his right side, who should extend his hand from one of the two sides to the cheekbone and to the division near the navel.
And then take a broad spatula and split the cheekbone from the middle location along its length near the temple.
And the incision should be made according to the rule equally distant from the line where the hair of the pubis begins, until it reaches the white line that encompasses it.
And peel that area, and observe, and be careful so that you do not incise it; and your peeling of the part which you are peeling should be suspended more deeply and carefully examine the peeling according to the quantity possible for you.
And then pierce the blister full of water with a wide puncture, and extract all the water.
And then separate between the two lips of the incision with forceps, and extend the blister to the parts above; and do not touch the skin that encompasses the pubis, and incise the blister in such a way as is possible for you. And incise it entirely or in pieces, and especially its soft part finely, for if you do not examine the incision carefully, there is no assurance that the water will not return.
But if you proceed with that outside of its skin during the time of your operation, after you have completed the incision of the blister, return inside it, and then adjust the two lips of the pubic skin with sutures, and then treat it as you would other wounds, until it is healed.
And if you find the wound corrupted by another illness, then it is necessary for you to ligate the vessels that are above the suspension, extract the wound, and then treat it as we have said.
But if the water is accumulated in two separate parts, then you know that there are two hernias; therefore, incise that part as you did with the other equally; and if it appears to you that an operation is necessary, then you should perform it.
After you have wiped away the blood, apply wool soaked in oil or rose oil to the sections; then place another layer of wool that has been immersed in wine and oil over both groins and the abdomen; cover this with folded cloths and poultices, and bind them all together in this manner:
Then take two cloths and fill them with crammed wool according to this pattern; make the extremities out of wood or wet wool, which are attached to the structure in the belt area of the pants; one end of an extremity should go into the buttocks towards the other, and the second should attach at the thigh part, and two extremities above two hips and two others above the knees, and all attachments should be adjusted in the belt area of the pants.
In some cases, this hernia is treated artificially with cautery at the site of the section using iron; you should take a thin knife-like cautery and insert it into the groin ligament, and ignite it as we have recommended until the white cicatrix containing the moisture becomes visible. Then you take a cautery in this form: and it is similar to the Greek letter.
Then pierce through that cicatrix, and ignite it until all the moisture is expelled.
Then extend the cicatrix with hooks and excise it with hooks, and cauterize it with a thin ignited cautery; incise it as much as you can until all of it is removed; be careful that the area does not catch fire from the heat.
And know that this operation is safer and more distant from bleeding, and better than an operation with cutting.
But if for you there is opposition in abscess during the time of cutting or cauterization, or bleeding, those times call for you to treat whichever opposition faces you accordingly, and with treatment that suits what was previously mentioned.

Chapter LXIII.

Chapter LXV.
On the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a section that exists in the cleft extending over the front part of the sides of the abdominal cavity, through which some of the bowels protrude.
And this rupture is either of the cleft or from its vicinity, and these two types occur for various reasons: either by impact, jumping, shouting, lifting some heavy object, or similarly.
And the sign of this, when it is due to an extension of the cleft, is that it occurs gradually over a long period of time, and does not happen suddenly, and it appears as an abscess equal in size to where the cleft compresses the bowel.
And the sign of it is when it is due to a section of the cleft, because at the beginning it causes a sudden and severe pain; and it appears as an abscess different from what would be seen near the rib, and this is due to the bowel protruding, and its body extends beyond the cleft.
And in some cases, the membrane is torn along with the bowel, and this type of intestinal hernia is called a strangulated hernia.
And in some cases, it is caused by gas, and in some cases the bowel forms a fissure where it is trapped, and this causes great pain to the patient, and often there is severe pain when pressure is applied.
And in the treatment of these types of this illness with iron, there is fear, because it is advisable to avoid the use of such instruments in this condition.

Chapter LXVIII.
On the softening of the cord of the testicles.
In many cases, the cord of the testicles is softened in some men because the flesh that is there is softened, and this requires careful attention.
