Chapter Second: On fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their figures vary, and the causes are distinct. Some fractures are caused by the impact of a hard object, and this impact removes part of the bone internally, which is behind the bone surface, just as it would split wood; thus, this type is called an axial fracture. Or the hard object cuts some part of the bone and cuts through the flesh but does not penetrate to the back side of the bone; this type of fracture is called absolute elevation.
And the severity of these two fractures can be great or slight, and there is also a fracture called comminuted or crushed; the cause of this is impact with something weak or falling on a stone, and similar circumstances.
And this fracture is either penetrating that approaches the panniculus which is below the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the severity of these two fractures may be great or slight.
And of the fracture that is weakened in the bone, in the subtlety of the small fibers, and for this reason this particular type of fracture is named capillary.
And this is the fracture that is caused either by falling or by being struck with a stone and similar objects, which causes some plate of bone to enter into the soft parts inside, and it occurs in the concave place just as it happens with the bones of animals when they are subjected to striking and result in their fragments entering into the inner parts.
And this is mostly done in the bones of human bodies, just as it happens with the heads of children.
And for all these types of fractures, treatments are applied, which are pieces just like small plates separated. And I will return to the reminder of all this in its place.
And you should recognize all these types of fractures through detection on them and by examining their indents and the discolored flesh that is over them.
And the capillary type of fracture is recognized by this: it appears on the bone and is wiped clean, and when examined closely, it appears as a small indentation, because the fracture shows up black.
But in the treatment of fractures, consider at the beginning the symptoms of the patient, and if you see from these symptoms that there is a clear indication of fear, such as vomiting with anger, extension, loss of understanding, hoarseness of voice, fainting, acute fever, and similar signs of distress and stiffness, and they resemble those in severe conditions, then doubt whether it can be cured.
And if for uncommon accidents you hope for a cure, then you begin the treatment of them.
Which cause is because if someone comes to you with a wound at the beginning as it is a wound and this is in the winter days, then it is necessary for you to study the condition of the bone until the day fourteenth according to all disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then it is necessary for you to wait and remove the bone before the seventh day, since what is above the bone may become corrupted by inflammation, and that accidents which we have recounted may occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the membrane covering the brain and is in small pieces and in a state of attrition, then it is necessary for you to incise the comminuted and abraded part, as I shall recount to you.
And you should shave the head of the patient who is wounded, and uncover the bone according to custom and method as best you can according to the shape of the wound, so that it is clear on the patient.

And so that you keep with all caution that the incisor does not touch any part of the thigh.
And when you have circled the perforated bone, and it is separated from the thigh if it continues there, and you will extract that, so that you rasp and equalize that in the roughness of what is on the bone with another tool similar to an incisor, but which must be finer and sharper than other incisors.
And if any part of the small and delicate bones remains, now take that with ease with what is suitable to you among the instruments; and then treat the wound with lint and dressings as we have said.
And the other method of operation is light, distant from fear, which Guy of Chauliac greatly praised; and this is its speech: It is necessary for you at the beginning to start uncovering the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe and obvious, until when you have uncovered that part and you have paused below it with the tip of this slow and joyful incisor, whose form is as follows:
And it is that the lens-shaped part is left intact and no part is incised, while the pointed part is divided into two sides along its length, as you see; that the lens-shaped part fits against the thigh with the sharp face of the pointed part facing the bone.
And then strike upon the incisor of one part with a small hammer until you have incised all the bone with ease as it is revolved, and be sure concerning the periosteum that no accident happens in any way that you fear, and even if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the least knowledgeable among them; certainly, and even if he were asleep.
If there is any reason to cut into the tissue of the neck in certain bone locations, incise with the sharp point of the lancet, and deliver it where it is easy to do so, since it should be done without harm or fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the tissue, and that which is abraded or remains of the bone is its surface, and appears rough with small protrusions, then it should be scraped and those protrusions removed with fine rasps prepared in various quantities so that you can apply them appropriately in any location during your operation on the broken bone and according to its shape.
And you should administer according to your proportion at which you scrape the bone initially with the coarser of those rasps, and gradually use finer ones, and do not cease doing this continuously until you reach administering the finest and most subtle of all of them.
But in each of the other small and fine fractures and subtle fractures, you should administer care according to what is needed for their correction.
And it is the reason that it is not feared by all those who have studied in that art. And thus regarding what we have written about major fractures.
And if the tissue is torn when you remove the bone, then you should take cloth according to the size of the wound, and soak it in warm oil, and place it over the wound opening; and then take another folded or triple-layered cloth and soak it in vinegar and warm oil, and place it on top of the first cloth as gently as possible without pressing too hard; and then apply a wide bandage over it, but do not tighten it beyond what is necessary to cover the wound.
If you are unsure about this treatment for one day or two until you are certain of an abscess that needs to be cauterized; and then untie it and promptly administer medicines that counter excessive dryness, such as lint pads and flour of oats, and powder of roses and aristolochia, and similar ones.
It is that you should make of these medicines powder that is finely ground over the wound, as they are dry; and generally, administer to it all medicine whose property is to cleanse without irritating.
And guard yourself according to your virtue so that the wound in your treatment is clean; and it should not be in the condition of pus or any other such state; nor should you allow its virulence to increase. For when it is adjusted over the panniculus cerebri, it corrupts that area, and you will damage it, and this malice will lead to further illness nearby.
And it affects the scalp, as when the bone is exposed and particularly when pressure is applied to the wound, and there is discoloration on its surface; and therefore consider these signs if you see the symptoms we have mentioned, know that it is dangerous without any doubt.
And if the discoloration is not due to the medicine that was applied above but rather because of the properties of that medicine, then you should take one part honey and three parts olive oil, and use these to moisten a piece of cloth, and promptly place it over the scalp; and then treat it with appropriate healing spices until it heals.
Chapter III.
On the restoration of the nose when it is cut.
Know that the nose is not broken except into two main parts, or sometimes into one of several pieces, because there are two bones there, for the lower part is cartilaginous, and it is not broken, but only crushed, twisted, and deformed.
If one of the two parts of it is broken, then you should insert your little finger into the socket of the nose, and reduce that fracture from within with your thumb inside and your fingers outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this with ease, and be careful not to cause pain to the patient during your procedure.
And if the fracture is in the uppermost part of the nose, and your finger cannot reach it, then you should use the end of a slender rod to reduce that fracture.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you should proceed as described.
And attempt to restore it on the first day of the fracture if possible; otherwise, wait until the seventh or tenth day when the swelling has subsided.
And insert at the end of the nose a pad of linen cloth if the fracture is in one part, or two pads if the fracture is in two parts, and let each pad be thick enough to fill the hollow of the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers say that the pads should be soaked in butter, and changed every day, but I have not seen this; rather, it is best to soak the pad in almond oil mixed with powder and leave it until the bone is set and the cartilage has hardened.
In some cases, a piece of cloth should be inserted into the nose, instead of a cauterization, wrapped in lion's skin; and it should be that with the fracture of the nose so severe that the patient is not prohibited from inspiration.
And this cause is not necessary for you; and if you want, do that, and if you want, do cauterizations.
And if another abscess occurs that needs to be cauterized, apply a plaster to the nose with pitch or resin mixed with vinegar and rose oil, or with myrrh.
But if an abscess that needs to be cauterized does not occur, now you should apply plasters there with sesame flour and powder of decocted herbs mixed with egg white; and then place them over the lion's skin, and do not treat the nose with any other cause.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or comminuted, now you should cut around them, and extract them with instruments that suit those cases; and then close the gap, and treat it with substances that promote healing and consolidation, and with appropriate plasters.
And if you encounter an infection inside the nose, now you should treat it with cauterizations and administer lead cannulas until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV.
On the restoration of the jaw when it is dislocated.
When the lower jaw is broken and there is no infection in the fracture, now you should check if the fracture is only on the surface, and not broken into two parts and curved inward, then its diagnosis is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, you should insert your left finger into the mouth of the patient; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left side, then insert your right finger; and push in such a way that the roughness of the fracture comes together easily from the outer parts with your other hand from the outside part of the bone; do this carefully.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two parts, then you should apply extension to both parts according to straightness as far as possible until the alignment is equal.
And if it happens that the teeth are dislocated or disrupted, then strengthen those which you expect will break with a thread of gold or silver or silk; and immediately place over the broken jaw some wax, and then place on it a doubled cloth, and place on that a large piece of linen well made, or a pad of soft material equal in length to the jaw; and then bind it above as is appropriate for you to apply bandaging and convenience dictates, so that it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and give him food in the form of thin soups.
And if you find that the figure is already altered in some way, then on the third day you should examine and loosen it; and then correct what has been altered of it with plaster made from powdered brick dust, egg white, or linseed flour, after you have removed the wax, and place over the plaster a thin layer of linen.
And then, as you place and adjust the plaster on it, if the position of the bone is not altered, leave it in place without loosening it until it is healed and the fracture is strong; for this fracture typically becomes strong in about three weeks.
And if another abscess should occur which requires cauterization, at that time administer what we have said about other similar conditions in the section on the treatment of the interior until it is healed, and remove that abscess.
And if the fracture is due to a blow, then consider whether the bone is separated or fragmented. At that time, use your skill to remove those fragments with what is necessary for their removal using the appropriate instruments.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then enlarge it with a scalpel as much as your needs require.
And when you have removed those fragments and not all can be extracted right away, then close the wound if it is large; and if not, apply one of the plasters that are suitable for it and let it heal until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
On the restoration of the collarbone when it is broken.
There are various ways the collarbone can be broken in front of the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three manners.
For it may be broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without fragments; and such cases are easier to restore. Or it is such that fragments occur in it, making its restoration difficult; or it is such that the fracture occurs due to a blow.
And the operation in such cases, when the fracture is due to a blow, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds the injured person and supports the broken collarbone, and the other extends the neck towards that part.
In order to treat this fracture, place padding as needed so that its shape fits appropriately, neither too high in prominence nor too deep in depression.
And if more extension is needed, now place beneath the tillic of the patient’s body a pad made of cloth or wool, and make its size according to your need; then extend and elevate the clavicle and compress the pad with your hand until the fracture is properly aligned.
And if you cannot draw the end of the clavicle outward towards the side, so that it reaches the preon, now have the patient recline on their neck, and place a pad over the muscle on one side, slightly larger in size.
And the assistant presses the muscle on one side below so that the bone of the clavicle is elevated to reach the preon, and then you correct the fracture and align it as described with padding.
But if you feel protrusions of the clavicle and small bones, now it is necessary to incise over them and remove those protrusions easily.
And if it is stuck in the bone, now you must devise an incision with one of the instruments that you have prepared for this purpose, after placing beneath the clavicle the tool used for rasping; and this tool is made of wood or iron, and has the following form:
And it is similar to the bowl of which this is the concavity; and its width should be according to your need for it, depending on the size of the bone or its smallness.
Make it as long as you possibly can for the operation; let it have two ends just as many times, and one end be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound from which you have incised when extracting the splinter from the bone is wide, and you are sure it will form an abscess, then you now sew together the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear it will form an abscess soon, then you fill the wound with patches and powders according to the quantity of the wound's opening.
And if an abscess forms soon, then infuse a patch in vinegar and rose oil, and place it over that.
And do not let the splint be loose over the broken bone; and so you should see the patient every day, and when the bandaging is soft or you see that the splint has already been moved, then you must correct this and tighten the bandage.
And place the patient's head on a pillow at the neck, and place below him a cushion where his head will rest during sleep for a short time; so that with that, raise the support under his armpit from that side, and thus elevate the fracture of the collarbone to the elevation of the muscle; or tie his arm to his neck.
And do not remove the bandaging unless you are in the immediate vicinity or there is an abscess, within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you have seen the face of it as such, and adjust in the constriction and restore that until it is restored and the fracture of the collarbone is healed.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the measure in twenty-eight days, and in some cases in less time for some people.
Chapter VI.
On the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The collarbone is broken at the middle part and not broken entirely but only the edges.
And when it is broken at that place — and it is broken in the middle part — and it is only known by touch, then according to the shape of the fracture prepare yourself and reduce it according to its natural figure in every way you can manage.
And after the powder of ground egg whites and lion's fat is applied, apply powdered cloth doubled over; and then place on the wound a bandage of fine linen according to the quantity of the spoonful all or at most a little more.
And if the bandage is convex in some places compared to the spoonful, then fill that convexity with lion's fat, and then press the bandage according to equality.
And then secure above with linen strips of appropriate length and with firmness of the bandage so that it is not displaced from its place.
And examine the dressing each day; and whenever it needs to be softened, change it and strengthen it and equalize the bandage if it has been displaced from its place.
And apply the medicine over the wound as appropriate.
And the spoonful is restored in 20 days or in 25 days.
And when this number of days is complete, then you loosen the dressing, and be sure that these are bones for which the beginning was not feared nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the place of the bone fragment and place a bandage below it, then cut above it, remove it, and act as we have said for the fracture of the collarbone or in case of suppuration, cauterize if it occurs here.
Chapter VII.
On the restoration of foot fractures.
The foot sometimes is broken in the middle and this happens quickly; however, the extremities of it move as cicadas do and are comminuted.
Of the accidents which happen when the bone is broken are that it inclines to the parts inside and causes great pain, difficulty in breathing, coughing, fever, and vomiting of blood; and it is evident in the cavity in the broken bone and is not hidden from feeling.
And the restoration of it is that the patient be laid on their back, and place a bolster between their shoulders; and then press the muscles, and adjust the ribs with two fingers, and carefully do the operation and the adjustment in every possible way and as is appropriate, until the figure of the bone returns as needed.
And then place on the plaster and tow, and place underneath fine spruce or linden wood or similar in lightness, since you wrap that in cloth; and then carefully bind that over the broken bone without pressure; and proceed with wrapping according to the roundness of the back several times, and tighten that with moderate tension; and then consider the bandage at all times, and when it has softened, tighten that again.
And if necessity compels the loosening of it due to corrosion or swelling, or pain, or an abscess that occurs in the area, then you examine and loosen that and remove the plaster and correct what has occurred according to the method of treatment; and then reapply the plaster, if it is necessary for this purpose; and adjust the tension until it is healed.
Chapter VIII.
On the restoration of the ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in thick places where they meet at the back; and from the extremities of them at the front part, such contusion does not happen to those because they are cartilaginous. And concerning the nerve, there is no deception regarding its sensation; therefore, you must examine it carefully.
And the restoration should be such that it equalizes the fracture as described according to the possible method, so that it is set properly, and then apply a plaster and bind the broken bone with a splint if you deem it necessary.
And if the fracture of the ribs inclines inwardly towards the parts inside, it causes severe pain and puncture-like sensations, just as occurs in one who weeps profusely, and because of this the bone places a covering; and there is difficulty in swallowing, coughing, and vomiting blood; and this condition is difficult to cure.
And the Ancients were skilled in that concerning living things.
And what they say is that you should avoid foods that cause severe inflammation and gas, so that the belly does not become swollen and distended, and thus the fracture is pushed outward.
But we should be cautious with this to ensure it does not become a light cause for generating an abscess, if it does not already happen; and if it does happen, then help is needed for that.
And some of them say that it should be stopped above the gas and then sucked out with force; and this reasoning is similar, but it is feared that the gas might draw the excess fluids to the area due to weakness.
And some of them say: the area should be covered with wool that has been dipped in hot oil, and place poultices between the ribs so that it is filled evenly, making the bandage equal as you wrap it according to its roundness; then treat the pain as one would for pleurisy with diet and medicine.
And if the patient is strongly constrained by the pain which they cannot bear, and the bone pushes against the dressing with a puncture point, we fear for the patient, at that time it is necessary to cut above the area, uncover the broken part, and immediately cease using any instrument that may damage the joint, the memory of which precedes, and that we should easily divide the bone and extract it.
And immediately adjust the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with stitching, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs internally, then you should incise it and apply powder in warm oil on the area, and treat the patient with what is appropriate for the internal abscess.
And let them be reclined on the side on which they sleep until they are healed.
Chapter IX.
On the restoration of fractures of the spine and the neck.
In some cases, fractures occur in the bones of the neck, and similarly in their healing, as well as in the spine of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if they will recover or not, then consider this: if you see their limbs already softened, numb, and dead, and they cannot move or stretch those limbs, and when you press them with your fingers or pierce them with a needle and there is no pain in them, then you know that they are not recovering, and in such conditions it is mortal.
And if they move those limbs and feel constriction when pressed and pierced with a needle, then you know that the bone is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see the vertebrae of the neck like this and want to know whether it will heal or not, then look at its condition.
Now if you see that the patient is already softened, and it happens in this way as it often does with many others, and soon after lying on their back they vomit involuntarily and have diarrhea, and sometimes when they try to lie on their back they want to urinate but cannot, then you will know that it is mortal, and at that point you should not labor further in its cure.
But if none of those causes occur, then the cause is better and more distant.
And the treatment for them is to apply poultices to reduce the abscess until you place on the affected vertebra olive oil that has been rubbed or boiled; apply this several times a day until the abscess is reduced.
And now apply one of the comforting and supportive plasters, and wrap it with a bandage; and instruct the patient to rest and remain calm and not to lie on the side where they feel pain until they are healed.
And if it happens that the bone is disjointed or for some other reason separated from its place, then you should set it in place and replace the bone; and then approximate the two sides of the wound, using stitches if necessary; and afterwards treat it with one of the healing ointments until it is healed.
But if the bone is fractured at its end and it is the tip of the bone, then you should insert your fingers into the buttocks, and align the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible using the second-degree reduction method; and afterwards apply a plaster and splint to it if needed, and wrap it securely.
And if you find broken bones in there, then immediately set them, and treat the wound with the treatment that is mentioned beforehand until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
On the restoration of the broad area/wound.
The bones of the broad areas are often broken, and when they break, the fracture is only communicated at the extremities of those bones and they split along their length and incline to the sides; and pain and numbness occur in the location, and movement is impaired when there is a fracture.
And the restoration of it is that you place your hand on it until it is set in the figure as it was before the fracture.
And if the fracture is at the extremity of it only, then you should align it as seems appropriate to you by equalization until it resembles its natural figure.
But if the fracture is in the long bone or one that has inclined to the sides, then you make the patient lie on their back until you are prepared for the restoration of that fracture. And when you have aligned it, apply a plaster over it, and then place a splint of wood or cane next to it, and bind it tightly with bandages so that there is no risk of puncture at the fracture site or displacement of the splint.
And align the concavity with what fills it until it conforms to the straightness equally, and instruct the patient to sleep on their back or side.
If an inflamed abscess occurs after cauterization, then you should stop the extension of it and its restoration until the cauterized abscess is calmed, and pause on what precedes that; and then return to the restoration of it, and strengthen it as necessary.
And if you encounter them in the bone or they are comminuted, then you treat them.
Chapter XI.
On the restoration of a fracture of the wrist.
The adjutorius is what is between the arm and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is fractured, then the restoration of it is in one of two ways, one of which is that you take a flat splint of moderate thickness according to this form:
And let them be attached at both ends with two bandages, and then it should be suspended from a high place.
And let the patient lie on a mattress; and then place the injured arm on the splint until it reaches the midpoint where the curvature of the splint is attached.
And then suspend something heavy on that, or allow it to extend downward; and then the physician should ensure that the fracture is kept as close together as possible, so that the fracture returns to its proper position.
And the other method is that the patient lies supine on their neck and suspends the arm at the neck with bandages; then command two assistants, one of whom holds what is above the fracture as tightly as possible, and the other holds what is below it; each should pull in their own direction.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then wrap one bandage around the fracture and another bandage over that, and each of the two assistants should pull on either end.
And therefore, if the fracture is near the end of the muscle, then place half of the bandage over the splint and the other half below the fracture, pulling it tight.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the joint, then place the bandage over that area and around the joint.
And wrap it in the same manner with ease and effort, and as soon as it is wrapped as needed and properly applied, then tighten it if no swelling occurs.
But if swelling occurs, then loosen the bandage within seven days, and place over it wool soaked in vinegar and rose water until the swelling subsides, and then tighten it.
And the method of tightening it is to place a plaster over the fracture, and then wrap a cloth bandage around the plaster; and then adjust the arm with the aid of an assistant, and have the hand resting on the side of the body.
And wrap the cloth and tighten it around the support so that the arm is in a fixed position, unless some cause related to the broken bone prohibits this.
And now use splints.
And it is that you place on the fracture the splint that is widest and strongest of the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and make the length of the splints according to the break with an addition of three fingers on all sides.
And place around the bones the bandage with the constriction which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandage over the place of the fracture be tighter there. And the farther apart the fracture is, the narrower should the bandage be.
And thus when you place the bones and the bandage as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its position, then do so.
And if you fear an abscess, then leave the bandage and the bones as they are until the seventh day as we have said.
And then consider the bandaging in all parts for three days so that no swelling or inflammation occurs in the place properly; or if food cannot reach the limb due to excess constriction; and thus correct everything as we have said.
But if you are confident that no cause for this will occur, then do not loosen the bandage until after three days or longer.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their arms be placed across their stomach.
And place a poultice filled with evenly spread wool, and at all times of the night or day ensure that the shape of the broken limb is not loosened or dissolved, or that the bandaging has become loose; therefore, correct this with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we mentioned before, such that the food is light at first until bandaging begins; and then gradually increase the nutrition of their diet.
Thus it is customary for the dressing and changing of it to be done every forty days; and at those times it should be loosened, bath given, and treated with medicines that correct it.
And if the fracture is comminuted, then you should not loosen it for either one day or even two months.
Chapter XII.
On the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the ulna and radius; one of which is small and is that which lies under the little finger, and the other is larger and is placed below the smaller one, towards the thumb.
And if it is the case that only the larger ulna or the smaller one is broken, or both are broken together.
And when the smaller ulna is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is quicker.
And when the ulna is broken below, the fracture is severe, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller ulna above, then it is appropriate for the physician, during its restoration, to allow a slight extension with ease until it is set.
But if the larger ulna is the one that is broken, then it is necessary to allow more vigorous extension.
And if both bone fragments are being cut, now it is necessary for you to apply the saw much more forcefully.
And it is necessary to position the hand in such a way that during the restoration and extension of the limb laid out on a board, the wrist should be at the top part, with all other fingers placed as they naturally would be, except for the little finger which should be lower than the others, and the patient should lie flat on their back.
And let there be a board in front of the patient at an appropriate height or level, so that the patient does not have to strain.
And let one attendant extend the arm from below toward the patient's hand or limb, while another attendant extends it similarly from above.
And let the physician then place the bone back into position as correctly as possible according to its proper alignment.
And if you encounter difficulty in setting the bone, now you must reduce all parts to their proper place with your skill.
And if there appears to be a complication separate from the difficulty that prevents the joint from being set and is not within your hope for restoration, now you should act upon it and remove it according to the method we have described above.
But if the fracture is due to an injury, I have already dedicated a separate chapter to this, so follow the treatment described there.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration process, now you should line a cloth with cerote made from rosin oil and white wax, which should be midway between thickness and subtlety, and apply this gently over the area where the abscess is present; then remove the cerote and apply a plaster prepared from powdered flowers mixed with egg whites, and subsequently place strips; let the strip that is placed over the fracture be further out and stronger.
And know that the number of stars on the arm is seen in the major dispositions, or that is, the fracture in one focus or in two focuses always.
And appropriately pause your traction on the site of the fracture forcefully and vehemently, and when you proceed with the traction above the fracture or below it, pause the traction a little more gently according to how it was preceded in the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that you wrap around the fracture should be soft and moistened, not hard or rough, and the bandage with which it is strengthened should be of medium thickness between fine and coarse, as we have recommended.
And consider the limb and the bandaging after some days. And if any cause arises for which correction is necessary, such as inflammation that occurs in the limb, then you should immerse the limb in hot water until the swelling subsides.
And let the limb not be constrained until it rests, and then resume the traction.
And if the traction has already been relaxed and the bone is already aligned as it should be, then correct all of that with your skill.
And consider whether nutrition is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive traction, for then you should relax that a little and let it be for some days until nutrition reaches it, and then strengthen it.
And if the patient does not experience any of the causes we have mentioned, then it is not necessary to release them for about 20 days or thereabouts.
And so the hand of the patient is suspended by its upper part for active movement, and make the patient lie on their side.
And know that this fracture is restored in xxx days or in xxxii days, or it is lightly restored in xxviii days, and all of this depends on the condition of the tissues and the strength of the patient.
Chapter XIII.
On the restoration of a fracture of the thumb and of the said bones.
Know that for the thumb of the hand and for the joints of the said bones, when they are fractured, they heal in about two months.
And when a fracture or dislocation occurs in the hand, it is necessary for the patient to lie flat and facing upwards according to equality, and then place the hand upon that extended.
And then the attendant should extend the broken bones and the physician should adjust them so that they are properly set.
And now it is necessary to apply plaster and cotton, unless an abscess requires cauterization; then place a pad above according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped that in linen.
But if the fracture is in the parts inside the palm, then make it appear as a ball of cloth, and instruct the patient to hold that with their fractured palm; then wrap it tightly with cloth.
And the pad should be made of soft cloth on which is placed something raised, so that it fits against the palm; and hold it tightly as needed.
If the fracture is in the bones of the forearm, place one splint on top and another under the part below in the palm, so that the hand may be opened and straightened; and then apply the bandages as if the hand were revolved at the wrist between the said splints.
And if it happens that there is a fracture in one of the bones mentioned, then if the finger is broken, bind it symmetrically as needed and then secure it to the palm; and if you wish, use a small straight splint so that the fracture is elevated and not compressed.
If the fracture is in any of the other fingers, such as in the middle finger, index finger, ring finger, or little finger, then bind it and attach it to the healthy adjacent finger; or secure all of them together continuously, which is better; or place a small splint over it, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your treatment, and also the position of abscesses that require cauterization and opposition—during your operation—as what is necessary when any cause of these occurs, as it is repeatedly described.
Chapter XIV.
On the treatment of fractures of the collarbone.
The collarbone, which is often fractured, is made evident by touch according to how it has shifted in the part in front and above.
And the treatment of it is done in such a way that one bandage is wrapped above the fracture and another below the fracture, and the patient lies prone on their stomach and face.
And so the surgeon should extend the bandage for its application according to the nature of the fracture when it is in the middle part of the bone.
And if it is closer to the waist, then the bandage should be applied from the hand to the upper part of the thigh, so that its extension ends there, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is closer to the hand, then the bandage should be applied from the hand, and its extension should reach the parts below.
And so the surgeon should equalize the bone on both sides but within limits that reduce it according to its natural appearance, and the bone is joined by proper conjunction; then it is necessary to apply plaster and bandage, unless an abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess does occur, then you should leave it for several days until the abscess has subsided, and afterward return to your treatment.
The bandaging should be done as follows: you need to wrap around the fracture a hard bandage, and fold it two or three times, and press out the excess; then wrap the thigh up to the waist.
And insert a long piece of cloth between the waist and the thigh near the hand at the lower parts, and let the ends of the cloth reach the most prominent part of both sides, and then roll over the thigh and waist any excess of the bandage.
And then apply strips of cloth over the waist at the location of the fracture, and place one of those strips over the bone of the thigh.
And fill the empty space between the hip bone and the thigh with soft cloths, so that the constriction may be coagulated.
And then begin the binding tightly from the middle at the site of the fracture with three or four layers, and as much as you distance yourself from the ligature site of the fracture, your binding should be looser, lighter, and softer.
And then extend the two ends of the bandage down to the lower parts until it reaches the nail of the foot, and tighten both ends of the splints on the other side so that the bandage is not displaced from its place.
And then place the binding over it in such a way that corrosion, abscess, swelling, or similar conditions do not occur on the limb; and if any cause of those should arise, immediately undo it and correct what has occurred as soon as possible, as we have said before.
But if in the bone there is a piece of bone that needs to be placed, you should then set it as best as possible; and if not, bandage over it and move it, and treat with whatever preceded the injury until it is healed.
And in some cases, this thigh fracture is tightened with splints as we have said regarding the assistant and the arm.
However, in the restoration of the limb done in such a manner, no lameness should occur to the patient; and if it is restored alone or with the application of the other hip, then it is necessary that the person will be lame permanently.
And know that the wound heals in forty days or a few months, and all this depends on the diversity of humors and other conditions.
Chapter XV.
On the restoration of a fracture of the lower jawbone.
Know that a fracture of the lower jawbone often occurs, but it rarely happens in the front part.
But if such a fracture does occur, it is usually just a crack or bruise in its parts, and this affects either one or several areas, and all of this depends on the sensation.
And the restoration of it is done in such a way that what is broken in its parts is aligned and joined as much as possible by means of adjustment, ease, and construction.
And then apply a plaster and place it over the round bandage if needed; let it heal above the structure as is appropriate.
And then consider all the conditions we have recommended for other fractures, just as in cases of abscesses and similar issues, adopt measures to prevent any accidents that might hinder healing until it is healed.
Chapter XVI.
On the restoration of a fracture of the chest.
Know that in the chest there are two bones, one of which is thick and is called the rib; the other is slender and is called the costal cartilage.
And it happens with some of the types of fracture that happen to both bones of the arm, and by this restoration is made just as restoration of the arm equally, and the operation of it is one.
And if you break both bones together, the curvature is distributed to all parts.
And if the more delicate bone is broken, the curvature is converted to the forward parts.
And if the thicker bone is broken, it affects that below, and it is of those causes that are not within your control.
And then administer extension and alignment and binding of the fragments according to that manner.
However, it is necessary if the fracture of the curvature is jagged with multiple fragments, that the extension be gentle and slower, and proceed with caution.
And in the curvature there is some cause for operation on the arm, and it is when you align the fragments and complete the entire procedure; at that time, you will take pieces of wood from a tree that is straight below its causes which are straightened by nature, and made similarly from palm branches; attach those to the ones with slight curvature, and not too thick, and let their length be according to the length of the curvature, and leave an entrance end towards the most distal.
And then wrap around each one a cloth in two layers according to the length of it, and place one at the side of the curvature on one side and the other on the opposite side, and let their ends enter at the lowest location of the body.
Between the two bones at three points of their extremities and in the middle point, because in this structure tilting to the right or left is prohibited, and it stands in a good position.
And now a piece of wood has been administered according to the length of the bone; and it is placed under the bone so that it maintains its ability to move.
And especially it is necessary that you do this in a fracture, and when it is properly a case of such an injury.
And each day consider the bone and study it carefully based on previous studies, and you must guard it from abscesses, inflammation, or other similar causes; and when any of these causes occur, then act accordingly until it is healed.
Chapter XVII.
Of the fracture of the shinbone and of the bones of the foot and of the ones mentioned.
A complete fracture does not commonly occur in the shinbone; but fractures do occasionally happen to the bones of the foot; and very few fractures occur to those of the foot, and they rarely result in injury unless there is a predisposition for it.
And if the bones of the foot are fractured and these bones are elevated above each other, then the patient should place their foot on the prepared ground as described.
And then place one foot on what is elevated by those bones, and then step on them, press, and bandage them until they return to their proper places.
And then apply a plaster and place it above, and below the arch of the foot, place a small board with two flat ends.
And approach with care that part of the body which is to be treated, since you have wrapped that area in cloths and other materials for the purposes required.
And after three or four days have passed, you then loosen the bandage, for you will find the bones either aligned, or broken or dislocated, and the procedure in these cases is as you know.
But if anyone says that they are broken, then you should restore them and align them as I have recommended to you in the restoration of the bones of the hand. And approach the broken finger with a board according to the length of the finger, and make it slightly longer; then place this board under the part as I have recommended, and secure it properly.
But if what is broken consists of two or three or more bones, then you should place a splint of appropriate length wrapped in linen on each finger, and secure the board below the base of the part, with one end of the board resting against the body so that it maintains good stability.
And it is important for you not to forget that you should consider all the causes mentioned to you in other fractures and accidents that we have discussed; and prepare yourself for any eventuality with what is necessary.
Chapter XVIII.
Of the fracture of the pubic bone in women and of the bone of the penis and the prepuce in men.
When the pubic bone of a woman is broken, then perform a surgical incision if necessary, and then slightly separate the two parts, with one side held back.
Let the midwife fill the container with cotton, and let it be made in the container as described; then let the woman be shaken, and raise the buttocks of the child little by little, for all this is intended to expel what remains inside until it is before the opening of the vagina as described and the fractured bone returns.
Then place poultices on the back of it, and when he wants to urinate, move the cotton aside to facilitate it, and then let him urinate, and reduce and return that according to the manner in which you mixed it at the beginning, and then return to his first poultice.
And do this for seven days or around that time, for he will be restored then.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and tie one end of a reed to its neck; then insert the entire bladder into his rectum, and then blow air through the reed vigorously until the bladder is inflated inside the rectum, for the fracture will return.
And then fill it with cotton and keep it for several days as we have recommended, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the pubis in a man or woman is fractured, then administer what we have recommended for the hip bone in its restoration and alignment.
And what is correct is not hidden in these nearby fractures that rarely occur, especially for one who has studied and practiced this and understands my book well; for such a fracture signifies to you in the major positions the right way in the restoration and in the constriction of it, and thus you understand well.
Take linen bandages when the penis is grasped, and place the penis in them; then press on its covering of cloth, and it should be wrapped up, and afterwards left for three days or thereabouts, and it will heal.
Chapter XIX.
On the restoration of fractured bones when inflammation occurs.
It is convenient for the one to whom it happens that a fracture occurs with inflammation, especially if the bone is large like the bone of the thigh or the humerus, or similar to them, that you should cauterize and blood-let him immediately if the conditions for blood-letting are indicated for him as we have said above.
And if the inflammation causes bleeding, then you should proceed with restraint in that case, such as powdering it with finely ground substances, if no other cause is present.
And then begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not delay it, unless an abscess occurs.
But if an abscess happens, then you should postpone the restoration until the ninth day, waiting for the abscess to subside, and do not approach it on the third or fourth day, for you would cause further harm.
But if the broken bone protrudes above the skin, then you should proceed to reduce it and equalize it with your hands as easily and with little extension.
But if extension is not prepared and the reduction of it with your hands, then you should reduce it with your hands using this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, its length being seven or eight digits, and its width according to the quantity of the inflammation; and for this reason, it is necessary for the physician to have three or four of these instruments in various sizes suitable for treating all kinds of fractures.
And let it be round, and let there be little grooves for so that it not be doubled over; place the compression upon it during the operation.
And let it have a sharp point at which there is a margin in its extreme parts; and let the upper part be thickening, and from the middle inward to the partitions.
And this is the form of it:
And it is called in Greek "labrum volans atelarem," which means a small palette.
So that the end of it may be raised up above the end of the prominent bone.
And press it into one notch between so that the bone returns to its place, and let it be equalized with some adjustment.
And thus strive to equalize the ends of the fracture between them, and if the end of the broken part is fine and does not take the instrument well by proper means, then it is necessary that you incise the end of that bone so that the instrument may have purchase on it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone entirely as we have recommended, then cut it with one of the cutting instruments which we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever manner is possible for you.
And smooth that which remains on the bone of roughness and small irregularities.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient feels intense pain in the tooth, then you know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and then do what will be of great benefit to the patient.
When you have completed the repair of the bone that you are performing, and now is summer, dip a cloth in black styptic vinegar, and do not apply cerate or any other substance containing oil to the wound, so that no corruption or putrefaction occurs there.
And immediately after you administer plates at the time of your completion of the bone repair, leave the wound uncovered so that you can excise any thrombus with forceps and use bandages according to the extent of the wound.
And be extremely careful not to allow the wound to become infected with the fracture, for many times this has happened due to the negligence of physicians, causing death or chronic illness in their patients, which is an injury. And if the swelling is slight, soft, and different from the swelling of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or is a bad wound, and you fear some of the harmful accidents that we have mentioned, and you feel pain in the area, then it is not necessary to use plates; instead, make an envelop with thick cloths rather than plates and secure it there.
And after one day or two, when the wound begins to generate granulation tissue, then you remove the cloth that you had placed over it with vinegar, and immediately apply medications, such as those we customarily use for wounds, including tetramericon and similar remedies.
And it is necessary for you to loosen the bandage and examine the wound every morning and evening until it is healed and firm, and it should be prepared in such a way that putrefaction can drain downward with ease.
If several days pass after the inflammation and it does not consolidate nor is a flap made of it from which to cut away the swelling, then you should know that here you are dealing with small veins, because you can probe into the inflammation. And of those small veins, those that are separate and can be extracted, you should remove them. For those that cannot be separated and cause pain when the limb is moved, try to incise them as much as possible according to what is feasible for you.
And if the inflammation becomes infected or ulcerated, which is a sign of serious corruption and putrefaction, then you should proceed according to one of those methods that are suitable for treating this condition, as previously described in its chapter.
And concerning the matter on which my speech rests and you should bring your attention: when a large bone is broken, it protrudes like the bones of the wrist and fingers and resembles those in larger limbs, and do not assume traction or extraction of it because doing so might lead to death after forty days; instead, leave it inside as it will likely fall out on its own within about twenty to thirty days. And then treat the inflammation if you see fit, otherwise leave it be.
Chapter XX.
On the treatment of knots that occur as a result of some fracture.
This knot often occurs as a result of the healing of a fracture, especially near the joints, and it is characterized by a deformation in the shape of the limb; it significantly hinders the normal function of the limb.
Then consider if the swelling is recent and administer medicines that have styptic properties such as aloes, myrrh, mirra (which may refer to myrrh or another resin), serpago (a medicinal herb), acrassia (likely a compound of vinegar and honey), and similar substances; take some or all of these. Confirm with one stiptic or white of egg or something agreeable, and apply this over the swelling in lint, and bind it firmly on it.
And let there be an uninterrupted compression so that you do not loosen it for several days; then untie it and reapply it until the swelling is reduced; or bind a lead plate over it gradually, since lead has the property of reducing everything that is inflamed in the limbs.
But if the swelling has already become hardened and is causing pain, and there is a necessity to remove it urgently, then cut into it at its highest point of pus, and incise the excess that is prominent, or reduce it with some instruments until it is reduced; and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter XXI.
On the restoration of a fracture when it is set and subsequently the limb is moved gently not contrary to its natural motion.
When a bone fracture is set and subsequently the limb is moved gently, this is done for various reasons: among which one reason is too much dissolution of the bandages and tying them not as needed; or excessive compression of the bandages preventing proper nutrition from reaching the limb; or excessive application of poultices; or improper movement at an inappropriate time; or lack of blood in the patient's body and weakness.
For treatment, give food to the patient and make his heart tender so that blood may be multiplied in him; administer baths; and apply cautery and ladanum on him.
And apply plasters to the limb so that the plaster attracts nutrition to it, or continue rubbing with rose water, until nutrition flows and returns to its natural form.
Chapter XXII.
On the treatment of broken bones when they have healed crookedly and prohibit the operation on them as it is not advisable.
When it happens to the limb that has already been restored near its healing from crookedness and prominence of the broken bone and knotting, and this is due to a change in the shape of the limb, but since the limb is not prohibited from its natural movement, then there is no need for you to accept the word of one who believes that member to be newly broken.
But there are already many false physicians and restorers who have done this in our land; and this operation is a shameful one, leading to great fear, the sign of which is fatal.
But if the limp and knotting are recent, then they should be rubbed with rose water in which soft herbs are soaked, such as mallow leaves and bark of elm, and melilot, and similar to these; and a plaster should be applied that is a softening plaster, well-made like dyallachion; or take mucilage of elm bark and mix it with barley flour or olive oil, and apply it as a plaster; or take fatty figs and grind them with dove’s milk, and similar preparations of medicines for strengthening consolidation.
And in some cases it is dissolved by frequent gentle friction which is applied to the most humid parts and movement of the member is administered to all parts at all times.
If tortuosity has already advanced and it has been made strong and converted into stone and provoked the necessity for treatment with iron, then one must ensure that the pus above is discharged and the continuation of the bone be dissolved, and let it be cut away what superfluous knots of that matter adhere to the bone with subtle instruments, and let ease be administered in this while solicitude and study are applied.
Chapter XXIII.
On the treatment of dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of any of the joints from its proper place, by which movement is prohibited, and the figure of the member is distorted, causing great pain and severe injuries.
When a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to act immediately to reduce it and not delay in doing so, for if it is delayed, the joint becomes inflamed and hardening occurs, making the reduction of the dislocation difficult.
And for this reason, it is not advisable to move or keep the patient standing during the period of inflammation, as movement often causes spasms and severe pain.
However, when this happens, it is necessary to prepare for the incision of the affected area, and then allow time for the inflammation to subside; afterwards, the limb should be bathed in warm water and oil, and then reduction should be attempted with ease.
And all members should be treated with what will aid their proper restoration to their place.
Here are the bones set in order according to what was stated about fractures; now I enter the part concerning lesser wounds.
Chapter XXIIIV.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
When both mandibles are dislocated and cannot be corrected immediately; the dislocation of both occurs in one of two ways: either they are slightly displaced from their proper positions through slight manipulation, and somewhat softened; or they are completely and fully dislocated such that they become softened at the joint where saliva flows from the mouth, and the person cannot close their mouth or hold anything with it, and the tongue is wrapped within the mouth.
But when the dislocation is slight, it often returns to its proper position on its own with little cause.
But if the dislocation is complete and full, it must be reduced promptly without delay.
And it is that the assistant hold the head of the patient, and the physician insert one thumb into the root of the dislocated jaw inside the mouth if the dislocation is on one side; or both thumbs together if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers rest equally outside; and instructs the patient to relax their jaws and allow them to move apart from each side; and the physician guides the jaw back into place until it returns to its proper position.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then apply warm water and oil compresses until a gentle reduction can be made for both without delay, as we have said.
And so when returning to the bandages, and the mouth of the wound is closed and not softened, then pause on those powders of cloth with cerot, and let it be made of wax and rose oil; and afterwards let it be applied with ease by the soft bands.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and his head be placed between his two ears, so that he does not turn to either the right or left; and do not force him to chew for any reason; let his food be pureed until such time as when the pains have subsided and his jaws are no longer swollen, then allow him to eat what he wants.
And this should be administered with ease, and he should not work against the opening of his mouth for eating and drinking and speaking, until such time as the jaw is healed and restored.
And if the reduction of both jaws when they are dislocated at any time and not returned to their proper places is difficult, then fevers often occur and frequent sweats, and very easily the patient's belly becomes swollen, and very easily he vomits pure bile.
And when you see this, then you know that it is hopeless; and very often he who suffers from this dies within ten days.
Chapter XXV.
On the Reduction of the Dislocation of the Clavicle and the Extremity of the Muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated from the chest below by its continuation to the arms; and sometimes it is dislocated outwardly, and this can be felt.
And for the restoration of the hand, the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. And then press the area with your palm firmly, for it will return.
And then apply the plaster and powder to that area.
But the extremity of that which joins the spine and is continuous with it is not dislocated on any day; therefore, it must be reduced and bandaged as we have said and according to how you have prepared it.
And then apply the plaster, powder, and bandage, and instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And in this method of treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter XXVI.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, one of which is when it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
And the second is when it is dislocated at the upper part of the muscle, and this happens rarely; and it is not dislocated at the rear parts by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated at the front parts by the nerves.
And it is most often dislocated and moves outward at the lower parts below the joint, and more frequently in those whose flesh is thin; whereas in those with thick flesh, it remains outside with difficulty.
If it happens that a blow or fall befalls any person, and the muscle of the limb becomes inflamed with inflammation, and is damaged because it has already been dislocated.
Then proceed with what you know while being very careful with it, and now proceed to its treatment.
And it is known that the dislocation of it occurs at the lower parts behind the nodule; therefore, you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the normal one, as you notice a clear difference between them; and you see in the head of the muscle a concavity and below the nodule you feel the head of the muscle like a chicken's beak, and the injured person cannot move their hand to their ear or use it for any movement.
If it is dislocated from the feet or above, then you will notice a clear and obvious touch, and it is not concealed.
And this dislocation is easily reduced when it is fresh and the injured person is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the practitioner raises the arm above the shoulder; then he places his fingers behind the nodule and elevates the joint with strength to its proper place, and the practitioner raises the arm, and extends it out straight; and then he keeps it still until it returns easily.
But if it does not return as we have said, and even after the initial days following the dislocation, then it is necessary to bathe the injured person in warm water and apply a poultice to soften and relax it as if for rheumatism, using ingredients such as rue seeds, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and administer it.
Let the patient lie supine on their back and place beneath them a cushion of wool which is even between softness and firmness. Then let the surgeon place his hand under the patient’s head, raising it to an upright position, and hold the patient's arm so that it is not injured behind, and another assistant should hold the patient's head so that it is not moved, for it immediately returns to its proper place.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this method: have a man present who is taller than the patient, and let him stand on the side where the affected limb lies. Insert your hand beneath the patient's thigh and raise the thigh upwards so that the patient is suspended in the air, and another assistant should hold the patient’s arm from behind their abdomen. But if the patient is robust, then it is necessary to cause them to be suspended by another means, so that they can bear it, because the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is reduced in another way: drive a long post into the ground with its top rounded like a millstone, neither too thick nor too thin, and place it beneath the patient; then lay strong cloths over the round part of the post. The patient stands along the length of the post, and extends one hand behind to support themselves; and stretch out the body on the other side with strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if the reduction is difficult due to all the causes we have mentioned, then use this treatment: take a post that is two ells in length, four fingers in width, and its thickness should be tapered at the point like this:
And over the round head place linen cloths so that the wood does not injure the patient.
And let the patient's neck be supported by pillows, and let the arm or the hand be extended along the board below, and let the board be wrapped around the helper and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
And let the arm be supported on a step of a ladder according to the latitude, and let the hand be extended below, and the other side be suspended from the other side, for the joint is within reach shortly.
And when the instrument is completed as is possible for you according to each manner, now it is necessary that you place below the neck a pad of woolen cloth of equal thickness; and then place plaster prepared with powder of moly and egg white over all the muscle, as has been described above, and then bind below the neck in an appropriate manner; and the bandage should be wrapped around the plaster, and the hand should be attached to the neck, and it should be left like this, and do not move the patient's hand for seven days.
And it is necessary that the patient's food be light until the limb is strengthened, for it is more conducive to their recovery.
And then food should be given more after seven or five days and one movement should be exercised; and when it is firm and not lax, then it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated due to swelling that occurs or for some other reason, then it is necessary to administer cautery with three tractions as described earlier in the chapter on cautery.
And if it is done as described and after seven days the bandage is removed and the joint is not fixed, and the plaster and strapping are reduced several times, and it cannot be raised and the arm cannot move upwards, then you know that the nerves which run to the end of the muscle have been cut or stretched or softened, and understand that the joint will never be set in its proper place.
Regarding the dislocation that occurs from the feet and the buttocks or the parts behind, the reduction is done with impulse and extension mostly, until it returns.
And let the other strapping be administered there, and care for it until it is healed.
And if heaviness and slowness in the movement occur near recovery, then the patient should use baths several times until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its original nature.
Chapter XXVII.
On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Neck.
Know that the joint of the neck is dislocated with difficulty and seems to return with difficulty; and it can be dislocated in all directions, but mostly forward or backward. The dislocation does not hide from you whether it is caused by a fall or some other contact depending on its figure, and when you adjust the dislocated neck with your hand, this is clearly shown to you because the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm, nor can it touch its own muscle.
And it is necessary that you treat the neck, and reduce the dislocation as soon as possible before it becomes an abscessed condition, for if it reaches the abscessed state, reducing it will be difficult, and even with much effort it may not heal completely. This is especially true when it involves the parts behind, because this type of dislocation is worse than all others in terms of severity and pain, and many times it leads to death.
And the restoration of it is such that if it can be reduced, then the hand should be extended on both sides and the arm should be expanded, and the physician's hands should be above the shoulder and below it, and he should press the joint with his fingers together, or with the palm of his hand until it returns to its place.
If the dislocation is to the front parts, then it returns thusly that he bends the hand with his hand, until he strikes the shoulder with the palm of his hand.
And if the dislocation does not obey reduction, then administer vigorous extension and strong movement.
And it is that two ministers extend the arm, and hold it in such a way that it is not retracted due to the extension.
And then the arm should be rotated in all directions, as it is wrapped with folded cloth long over the hand, or bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that this aids in the lubrication of the joint for ease; and then press the joint with vigorous pressure until it returns.
And then the reduction should be followed by resting on a bandage that is firm and supportive over white cloth, and he should be wrapped with tight bandaging, and the arm suspended from the neck, and left for several days.
And then it should be loosened; and if the joint is formed in its proper place, then you loosen the bandage of it and leave it.
And if you see that the joint should not be stitched together, then now apply the plaster and bandage, and leave it like that for several days until it is healed; and afterwards loosen it.
And if it happens that there is great pain around the joint reduction and the disposition of the limb is in motion, then administer moistening through bathing and subtle friction, and compression until it is calmed; or place upon the joint something cold, which is an antidote to violent movement; and afterwards bind it, and leave it like that on the bone day and night. And afterwards remove that, and put it in a bath; and when it is soaked, then you should rub the joint with moderate friction, and afterwards apply something cold to it, which is an antidote to movement, once, and the second time, and the third time, in a bathing instrument until it is soothed.
And if you wish, place upon it a warm and moist cow's hide piece at the edge and stitch it; and do this several times, for it is moist and reduces to its proper disposition.
Chapter XXVIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the elbow joint, which is the joint between the arm and the hand. Note that there seems to be a slight inaccuracy in describing the joint as "between the arm and the hand," typically it refers to the elbow joint between the upper arm and the forearm. However, I have translated according to the provided text.
When the hand is dislocated in some cases, the reduction of the dislocation is easy, and the opposite is true for other joints; however, it is necessary to act with speed in reducing it at the hour when it was dislocated, before swelling occurs or a harmful condition arises.
And the reduction of the dislocation of the hand is that you place the joint of the patient's hand on a table and the assistant extend the patient’s hand, and the surgeon places his hand or his palm upon the vicinity of the joint, and push it in until it returns to its place.
However, it must be considered whether the palm is already displaced toward the back of the hand; then the patient places the back of his hand on a table during the extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is towards the back of the hand, then the position of the hand should be entirely on the table so that the surgeon’s hand can lie upon its vicinity.
And if it returns quickly, that is good; and if not, then bind it firmly while the swelling subsides and leave it as such; and do not repeat this, for otherwise, it will not endure nor can you proceed with the reduction of the swelling beyond these days.
However, the joint remains stiff according to its tortuosity; and do not inform the patient of any cause, but say that the hand is relaxed and cannot be bound for any reason; then you will know that the nerves are injured or severed, and at this time there is no remedy except to bind it with cauterization near the injury and strengthen it by applying some substance.
And so when it has been returned, then place on it the bandage which we have recommended, and let it be left bound for five days; and afterward let it be loosened and left, and the hand should be exercised.
However, if the movement of it is restricted and you encounter any gravity in it, then moisten that with warm water and apply friction several times until it is relieved.
Chapter XXIX.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the said joints.
In some cases, the said joints may be dislocated completely.
And when any of them is dislocated towards the back of the palm or towards the belly side, then hold the finger still and gently push it back into place with your fingers until it returns.
And then hold the tip of the finger, and suspend that part which is dislocated, and leave it like this for two days.
And then suspend that part and extend it until it is properly bandaged on that day.
And when it is night, wrap that part according to the narration, and do not stop unwrapping it during the day and exercising with movement, and wrap it at night; and do this for several days until it is straight.
And do the same if it is dislocated towards the palm side, and wrap it according to that method and perform the first operation in this way until it is healed.
And do the same when it is dislocated to other parts as well.
Chapter XXX.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae in the back.
When one of the vertebrae in the back or neck is completely dislocated and the bones move from their positions, then there is no cure for this, as death is feared for the patient.
This is the sign that the patient voids without control and cannot retain it; often two or three fingers can soften certain parts of him or both sides of him or both arms of him, or one of these; or his vertebrae are displaced, because some are displaced and some are moved away from their place in four directions.
Thus it is displaced to the rear parts and is called herniation.
But the treatment for him is to consider whether the herniation occurs during childbirth, because then there is no cure and he will not be healed from that.
But in the case where it happens due to a fall or a blow or something similar to these, the Ancients have described the methods of treatment at great length, from which I will return only what is essential; but I have abridged that which is lengthy on account of my closeness to understanding and exposition, with which I have explained and formulated this from various instruments different from those by which I would show this and expand upon that.
And none of those is cured except the one that occurs properly as I have mentioned.
And it is that the patient lies on his back near a wall, extended below with a soft cushion so that his feet are not strained.
And nearby a straight pole should be placed, planted in the ground at one end near his head, and another pole around his feet at the other end of the cushion.
The minister holds the staff, and let it not be fixed in a hole; another minister holds the other according to that form.
And then wrap a bandage over the patient’s head and let it gently press against it; extend the end of the bandage to the staff that supports the head and tie it there; then place another bandage across the patient's hips, covering their buttocks; and finally adjust all the bandages and secure them to the other staff that supports the feet.
And the minister should extend the bandage to the part of the staff; however, do not move the two staffs and their respective bandages from their places once they are positioned, as we have said.
And the physician should place his palms on the strong part of the spine until it returns to its proper position, or he can place the patient on a table and then adjust the patient’s feet until they return to their proper position.
But if it does not return to its proper position by this method, then take a table whose width or length is about three cubits, and dig into the wall near the patient a place where part of the table can fit. And then place the middle of the table on the spine, and have the physician stand on one end of the table while the patient's feet are positioned on the other end, and pull gently until the spine is compressed and returns to its proper position.
And if you wish, open the part that is twisted with your hand; and place yourself on the ground behind the head of the patient between the ends of two staffs, each one arm’s length, and behind the head of the patient another two staffs in the same manner. The distance between each staff should be a palm, and there should be a hole bored through each staff where it is twisted, and each should be securely fixed to the ground so that they do not move.
Let a round rod be inserted into the tube, and let it be rotated in the two grooves of the two rods.
And at the end of it is a groove in which the rod is formed, the length of which is one palm and a bit that is rotated; and in the two other rods there is something similar to that.
And bind tightly the bandages that you have stretched on the feet of the patient in the groove which is behind it, and the bandages that you have stretched on the thighs of the patient in the ring which is behind the heels.
And behind each ring should be a minister who turns the ring with his hand, and the surgeon likewise moves the spine as we have said.
And after the spine is returned and the place is adjusted, it is now necessary to apply an astringent paste mixed with egg white, and then cotton; and on top of the paste, place strips of oak bark whose width is three fingers or around that size, and the length should cover the area of the inflammation, and extend over the part of the spine.
