And then bind it with the bandages as we have said, and let the patient use delicate nourishment until he is healed.
And if there is any inflammation in the area at the end of the healing process, it is now necessary to administer medicines that soften and soothe, as recommended for long-term use on the affected area; and a piece of lead may also be applied to that location.
And in some cases it happens that there is an impingement on the vertebrae of the back, and this is caused by that dislocation; and that bone which already protrudes should not be treated with this medicine, for it would easily cause your death.
Chapter XXXI.
On the Treatment of a Dislocated Hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the muscle attachment suffer only from dislocation, and do not experience what other joints with small movement and concavity might endure.
And the joint of the hip can be dislocated in four ways, for it can be dislocated inward, outward, forward, or backward.
And it is most commonly dislocated inward, and rarely forward or backward.
And the sign of dislocation towards the inside is that when you measure the leg of the affected side against the sound side, it is longer and the heel is more prominent than on the sound side, and the patient cannot bend their thigh past a certain point, as if there were an abscess in the area where cautery would be applied for such conditions, because the head of the bone has already reached this location.
And the sign of one who has suffered dislocation towards the outside is that the symptoms are contrary to those mentioned above.
And the sign of one who has suffered dislocation towards the front is that when they try to straighten their leg properly, they cannot bend it without pain in the heel, and if they attempt to walk, they cannot do so forward; and their urine is retained, and their groin becomes swollen with an abscess, and they must walk on their toes.
The sign of the one to whom a dislocation has occurred in the posterior parts is that he does not expand the joint, nor can he bend it before bending the elbow, and the curve of it is shorter than the other, and the elbow is softened, and the end of the bone is out of place.
The main reduction of the species of this dislocation is that you consider whether the dislocation is old and ancient, in which case it has already been accustomed to being there. You then administer reduction and maintain it according to its position; however, at that point, it is not entirely cured, and therefore it should not be expected to be fully restored.
In the case of one whose dislocation is recent and belongs to one of the four species of dislocation, you should attempt to return the joint by extending it inward and outward, moving it from side to side, for it easily returns without needing further treatment.
However, if it does not return in this manner, you should prepare a strong assistant and extend the curve of the joint from behind, either by hand or with bandages placed over the joint above the elbow. Another assistant should extend the arm so that their hands are on the sides of the joint. Then, tighten the bandage tightly over the limb, and have another person hold the end of the bandage.
And this extension should be from the joint or the inner part to the angle near the collarbone, or from the rear part to the side part.
And this extension should be done in one motion while the patient is raised off the ground and kept hanging.
And this kind of extension is common to all four.
And if the dislocation returns as we have said, it is good; and if not, it is now necessary for every kind what I will say about its proper treatment.
In the reduction of its own, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is towards the inner parts, it is necessary for the patient to lie supine on their back; then place the bandages over the ridge of the breastbone at the point where the head of the collarbone and the part below the clavicle meet; then extend the bandage from under the armpit to the farthest part of the shoulder blade.
And then another strong minister should take hold of the arms, and firmly grasp the swollen area of the dislocated collarbone and extend it outward forcefully, for that will return to its proper place.
And this kind is easier to cure than the others when this member is reduced.
But if it is excused on you and you do not obey the regimen for this kind of treatment completely, then it is necessary to bind both sides of the patient tightly with a strong, lightweight bandage over both heels and both toes.
And let the length of each be four fingers, and let the injured limb be extended more than the other by a quantity of two fingers.
And then the patient should be suspended at the top of the post and held by the hand; and let the distance of the patient from the ground be two arms' lengths.
Here is the translation:
In the proper reduction, when there is a dislocation to the outer part, have the patient lie on their back as we have recommended in the yellow book; and tightly bind the limb affected by the injury and its foot.
And place two boards, one under the feet and the other under the head; and place adjusted boards on both sides of the thigh placed upon it, wrapped in damp cloths, so that the patient is not damaged by the board being between their parts, so that the extension to the lower parts is not pulled.
And each assistant extends to their part, and the surgeon with his hand performs the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is to the front parts, then it is necessary that the limb be extended quickly, and this method of narration applies while lying on the back; and the surgeon places the palm of his right hand on the affected joint, and with that compression uses the other hand to extend it to the lower parts toward the side.
And if the dislocation is to the rear parts, then it does not require that the patient be extended to the lower parts, and they are elevated from the ground; instead, they should be placed on a hard surface, as required in the same manner if the hip is dislocated to the outer part, according to what we have said about positioning the patient between two boards; and it is on their face with the limbs extended, as we have said, without harm.
And let there be applied compression with a bandage over the place where you are joining.
Thus, when the reduction is complete as it should be — and the sign of proper reduction is not hidden from you, which is that you extend the patient's limb and when you see them equal and the affected part extends its normal position and expands for its excuse, then you know that the limb has returned as it should — and then you adjust two things and pause with bandaging so that the width is not diminished in any of the parts; and adhere the plaster to rest for three days or four.
And then loosen the bandage and the plaster, and compare one limb with the other.
Now, if you see them equal in appearance, then you know that the dislocation is already fixed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any cause for softening, then you return and bandage that area, and extend it as in your initial structure, and let it rest for three days.
And then loosen that area, and have the patient delay in walking on it for several days until it is strengthened.
Chapter XXXII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the knee.
The knee can be dislocated in three ways: it can be dislocated to the outer parts, to the inner parts, and to the lower parts or behind; but it cannot be dislocated to the front parts.
The sign of a dislocation is that you order the patient to apply pressure with their hand on the joint; at this time, you know that the bone is dislocated.
And the restoration of all types of dislocation is that you make the patient lie straight, so that they extend their hand; adhere your chest to the side of them, and place your feet between their legs; then immediately adhere the bone with your palms, and hold those between your fingers around the bone firmly, and another assistant extends their foot within so that the bone returns to its place.
The sign of reduction of the dislocation is that the patient's hand adheres to the joint without laborious effort.
And then apply a plaster to that and make the hand adhere to the joint, and immediately bind those together with a bandage for three days or four, and then loosen them.
And the patient should not use movement for several days until they are healed and comfortable.
But if the reduction of it is unnecessary for you, now administer extensive treatment with bandages as described in the preceding narration about the cure of the hip.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the thigh.
The thigh sometimes is moved by slight displacement, and sometimes is dislocated completely, and the dislocation of it occurs either towards the inside or towards the outside.
And the sign of the dislocation of it is that you see the thigh swollen towards the side to which it is dislocated.
The treatment for the removal of what is superficial should be easy and gentle, and it should be gradual so that it returns easily.
In the treatment when it is dislocated for a second time, you must make the patient lie straight, and have someone strong and capable stand behind him at the midpoint of his body.
And place your right hand on the most superior part of the foot, and your left hand on the lower part of the foot at the location of the ankle.
And pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with your left hand for two attempts, and then extend it with your left; and press the foot’s sole with your right hand gently while working.
And do this two times as we have counted.
And then press the foot’s sole gently with your hand for the third time, and you should be at the ankle.
Therefore, if it returns in one attempt or two following this method, and if the foot is placed gently, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation, for it will return.
But if you are prohibited from reducing it as we have recommended, then make the patient lie on their back on the ground, and place a padded roller firmly on the ground, which fits between the affected parts, and you should wrap the area with cloth so that the patient does not feel discomfort.
And then have one assistant hold the patient's limbs, and another assistant extend the foot with their hands or with straps placed around the neck of the foot; and then each assistant should stretch it according to their position, and the roller should be extended between the patient’s parts, holding them in place so that the body does not pull back downward during the extension.
And so the surgeon holds the displaced bone with his hands, and another assistant holds the healthy bone in position below, for the displacement returns easily.
And so when the displacement returns and the health of the reduction is declared to you, pause and apply a plaster and bind it tightly with strong bandages, and tie the limb to the bed.
And be careful to avoid the nerves which are behind on the heel, so that the bandage over them is not too tight and does not injure them severely.
And leave that for two days or three; and if the bandage is loosened, bind it again.
And then loosen it on the third day or fourth, and forbid the patient from moving for forty days. But if the patient is forced to move before that time, without ensuring that the displacement has not dissolved and become corrupted, then it will receive no cure.
And if an abscess requires cauterization, then you should proceed according to the instructions in our narration on that subject, in another section concerning treatment and application, until it is healed.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the treatment of dislocation of the said feet.
And let the one who is dislocated be handled with some extension without labor; and what is not difficult is rather easy.
But if the dislocation is in any of the joints of both feet, then you should have the patient lie down with his foot on a padded surface on the ground or on a table, and be positioned as if standing.
Here you should stand at a distance, and pause the hand on what is above the joints, and then press that part with the hand vertically until it returns, so that you see the swelling equalized and not appear in one prominent location.
Then pause and place the foot on a table that holds the entire leg, which has two ends, and then press that part with proper tension and firmness for three days; then loosen it and allow rest from walking for ten days, until it is pressed and recovery is assured.
Chapter XXXV.
Of the kinds of dislocation that are made with injury or fracture or any combination thereof.
When some cause of those occurs, and you commit to the treatment of it and the restoration of the limb, often such cases lead to death.
And because of this, one should not presume to treat it themselves but rather seek someone who is wise in the art, experienced for a long time, merciful and pious, cautious, not rash nor presumptuous; and use at the beginning medicines that reduce boils slightly, and leave the patient to divine judgment, except for one to whom you hope for salvation from peril, or relief from illness, where there appears some hope. And then strive to immediately perform the reduction of it at the beginning of the cause, before it becomes severely inflamed.
And then if the limb returns as we wish, administer a regimen that reduces severe inflammation and treats the injury with what is appropriate from among the dry remedies.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and it happens in the bones that are separated, then you perforate their membranes, and do what we have said about the simple diseases concerning what precedes in those places.
And guard and avoid according to your virtue that which involves the instruments in the ways of fear as has been preceded in my preaching; for this is better for you to serve your Creator, and more permanent for your glory, and more salutary for your true body, which will be perceived in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis perfectly; may God be blessed for them. Amen.
